<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1316064257">.mw-parser-output .infobox-subbox{padding:0;border:none;margin:-3px;width:auto;min-width:100%;font-size:100%;clear:none;float:none;background-color:transparent;color:inherit}.mw-parser-output .infobox-3cols-child{margin:-3px}.mw-parser-output .infobox .navbar{font-size:100%}@media screen{html.skin-theme-clientpref-night .mw-parser-output .infobox-full-data:not(.notheme)>div:not(.notheme)[style]{background:#1f1f23!important;color:#f8f9fa}}@media screen and (prefers-color-scheme:dark){html.skin-theme-clientpref-os .mw-parser-output .infobox-full-data:not(.notheme)>div:not(.notheme)[style]{background:#1f1f23!important;color:#f8f9fa}}@media(min-width:640px){body.skin--responsive .mw-parser-output .infobox-table{display:table!important}body.skin--responsive .mw-parser-output .infobox-table>caption{display:table-caption!important}body.skin--responsive .mw-parser-output .infobox-table>tbody{display:table-row-group}body.skin--responsive .mw-parser-output .infobox-table th,body.skin--responsive .mw-parser-output .infobox-table td{padding-left:inherit;padding-right:inherit}}</style><table class="infobox" style="float: right; width: 25em;"><tbody><tr><th colspan="2" class="infobox-above">Banana</th></tr><tr><td colspan="2" class="infobox-image"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Bananavarieties.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Bananavarieties.jpg/250px-Bananavarieties.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="143" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/de/Bananavarieties.jpg/387px-Bananavarieties.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/d/de/Bananavarieties.jpg 2x" data-file-width="387" data-file-height="221" /></a></span><div class="infobox-caption">Fruits of four different <a href="/wiki/List_of_banana_cultivars" title="List of banana cultivars">cultivars</a>. Left to right: <a href="/wiki/Cooking_banana" title="Cooking banana">plantain</a>, <a href="/wiki/Red_banana" title="Red banana">red banana</a>, <a href="/wiki/Latundan_banana" title="Latundan banana">apple banana</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Cavendish_banana" title="Cavendish banana">Cavendish banana</a></div></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label">Source plant(s)</th><td class="infobox-data"><i><a href="/wiki/Musa_(genus)" title="Musa (genus)">Musa</a></i></td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label">Part(s) of plant</th><td class="infobox-data">Fruit</td></tr><tr><th scope="row" class="infobox-label">Uses</th><td class="infobox-data">Food</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>A <b>banana</b> is an elongated, edible <a href="/wiki/Fruit" title="Fruit">fruit</a>—botanically a <a href="/wiki/Berry_(botany)" title="Berry (botany)">berry</a><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Morton-2013&#95;1-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Morton-2013-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>—produced by several kinds of large treelike <a href="/wiki/Herbaceous" class="mw-redirect" title="Herbaceous">herbaceous</a> <a href="/wiki/Flowering_plant" title="Flowering plant">flowering plants</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Genus" title="Genus">genus</a> <i><a href="/wiki/Musa_(genus)" title="Musa (genus)">Musa</a></i>. In some countries, <a href="/wiki/Cooking_banana" title="Cooking banana">cooking bananas</a> are called plantains, distinguishing them from <b>dessert bananas</b>. The fruit is variable in size, color and firmness, but is usually elongated and curved, with soft flesh rich in <a href="/wiki/Starch" title="Starch">starch</a> covered with a <a href="/wiki/Peel_(fruit)" title="Peel (fruit)">peel</a>, which may have a variety of colors when ripe. It grows upward in clusters near the top of the plant. Almost all modern edible seedless (<a href="/wiki/Parthenocarpy" title="Parthenocarpy">parthenocarp</a>) cultivated bananas come from two wild species – <i><a href="/wiki/Musa_acuminata" title="Musa acuminata">Musa acuminata</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Musa_balbisiana" title="Musa balbisiana">Musa balbisiana</a></i>, or their <a href="/wiki/Hybrid_(biology)" title="Hybrid (biology)">hybrids</a>.
</p><p><i>Musa</i> species are native to tropical <a href="/wiki/Indomalaya" class="mw-redirect" title="Indomalaya">Indomalaya</a> and <a href="/wiki/Australia_(continent)" title="Australia (continent)">Australia</a>; they were probably <a href="/wiki/Domestication" title="Domestication">domesticated</a> in <a href="/wiki/New_Guinea" title="New Guinea">New Guinea</a>. They are grown in 135 countries, primarily for their fruit, and to a lesser extent to make <a href="/wiki/Banana_paper" title="Banana paper">banana paper</a> and <a href="/wiki/Textile" title="Textile">textiles</a>, while some are grown as <a href="/wiki/Ornamental_plant" title="Ornamental plant">ornamental plants</a>. The world's largest producers of bananas in 2022 were <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a> and <a href="/wiki/China" title="China">China</a>, which together accounted for approximately 26% of total production. Bananas are eaten raw or cooked in recipes varying from curries to <a href="/wiki/Banana_chips" title="Banana chips">banana chips</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fritter" title="Fritter">fritters</a>, <a href="/wiki/Fruit_preserves" title="Fruit preserves">fruit preserves</a>, or simply baked or steamed.
</p><p>Worldwide, there is no sharp distinction between dessert "bananas" and cooking "plantains": this distinction works well enough in the Americas and Europe, but it breaks down in <a href="/wiki/Southeast_Asia" title="Southeast Asia">Southeast Asia</a> where many more kinds of bananas are grown and eaten. The term "banana" is applied also to other members of the <i>Musa</i> genus, such as the <a href="/wiki/Musa_coccinea" title="Musa coccinea">scarlet banana</a> (<i>Musa coccinea</i>), the <a href="/wiki/Musa_velutina" title="Musa velutina">pink banana</a> (<i>Musa velutina</i>), and the <a href="/wiki/Fe%27i_banana" title="Fe&#39;i banana">Fe'i bananas</a>. Members of the genus <i><a href="/wiki/Ensete" title="Ensete">Ensete</a></i>, such as the <a href="/wiki/Ensete_glaucum" title="Ensete glaucum">snow banana</a> (<i>Ensete glaucum</i>) and the economically important <a href="/wiki/Ensete_ventricosum" title="Ensete ventricosum">false banana</a> (<i>Ensete ventricosum</i>) of Africa are sometimes included. Both genera are in the banana family, <a href="/wiki/Musaceae" title="Musaceae">Musaceae</a>.
</p><p>Banana <a href="/wiki/Plantation" title="Plantation">plantations</a> can be damaged by parasitic <a href="/wiki/Nematode" title="Nematode">nematodes</a> and insect pests, and to <a href="/wiki/Fungal_infection" title="Fungal infection">fungal</a> and <a href="/wiki/Bacteria" title="Bacteria">bacterial</a> diseases, one of the most serious being <a href="/wiki/Panama_disease" title="Panama disease">Panama disease</a> which is caused by a <i><a href="/wiki/Fusarium" title="Fusarium">Fusarium</a></i> fungus. This and <a href="/wiki/Black_sigatoka" title="Black sigatoka">black sigatoka</a> threaten the production of <a href="/wiki/Cavendish_banana" title="Cavendish banana">Cavendish bananas</a>, the main kind eaten in the Western world, which is a <a href="/wiki/Triploid" class="mw-redirect" title="Triploid">triploid</a> <i>Musa acuminata</i>. Plant breeders are seeking new varieties, but these are difficult to breed given that commercial varieties are seedless. To enable future breeding, banana <a href="/wiki/Germplasm" title="Germplasm">germplasm</a> is conserved in multiple <a href="/wiki/Gene_bank" title="Gene bank">gene banks</a> around the world.
</p>
<meta property="mw:PageProp/toc" />
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Description">Description</h2></div>
<p>The banana plant is the largest <a href="/wiki/Herbaceous" class="mw-redirect" title="Herbaceous">herbaceous</a> flowering plant.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-INIBAP-2000&#95;2-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-INIBAP-2000-2"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>2<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> All the above-ground parts of a banana plant grow from a structure called a <a href="/wiki/Corm" title="Corm">corm</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEStoverSimmonds19875–17&#95;3-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStoverSimmonds19875–17-3"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>3<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Plants are normally tall and fairly sturdy with a <a href="/wiki/Tree" title="Tree">treelike</a> appearance, but what appears to be a trunk is actually a <a href="/wiki/Pseudostem" class="mw-redirect" title="Pseudostem">pseudostem</a> composed of multiple leaf-stalks (<a href="/wiki/Petiole_(botany)" title="Petiole (botany)">petioles</a>). Bananas grow in a wide variety of soils, as long as it is at least 60 centimetres (2.0&#160;ft) deep, has good drainage and is not compacted.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEStoverSimmonds1987212&#95;4-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStoverSimmonds1987212-4"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>4<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> They are fast-growing plants, with a growth rate of up to 1.6 metres (5.2&#160;ft) per day.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-5" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-5"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>5<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The leaves of banana plants are composed of a stalk (<a href="/wiki/Petiole_(botany)" title="Petiole (botany)">petiole</a>) and a blade (<a href="/wiki/Leaf#General_characteristics_of_leaves" title="Leaf">lamina</a>). The base of the petiole widens to form a sheath; the tightly packed sheaths make up the pseudostem, which is all that supports the plant. The edges of the sheath meet when it is first produced, making it tubular. As new growth occurs in the centre of the pseudostem, the edges are forced apart.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEStoverSimmonds19875–17&#95;3-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStoverSimmonds19875–17-3"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>3<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Cultivated banana plants vary in height depending on the variety and growing conditions. Most are around 5&#160;m (16&#160;ft) tall, with a range from '<a href="/wiki/Dwarf_Cavendish_banana" title="Dwarf Cavendish banana">Dwarf Cavendish</a>' plants at around 3&#160;m (10&#160;ft) to '<a href="/wiki/Gros_Michel_banana" class="mw-redirect" title="Gros Michel banana">Gros Michel</a>' at 7&#160;m (23&#160;ft) or more.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-RHS-2023&#95;6-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-RHS-2023-6"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>6<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEPloetzKeplerDaniellsNelson200712&#95;7-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPloetzKeplerDaniellsNelson200712-7"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>7<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Leaves are spirally arranged and may grow 2.7 metres (8.9&#160;ft) long and 60&#160;cm (2.0&#160;ft) wide.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Morton-2013&#95;1-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Morton-2013-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> When a banana plant is mature, the corm stops producing new leaves and begins to form a flower spike or <a href="/wiki/Inflorescence" title="Inflorescence">inflorescence</a>. A stem develops which grows up inside the pseudostem, carrying the immature inflorescence until eventually it emerges at the top.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEStoverSimmonds19875–17&#95;3-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStoverSimmonds19875–17-3"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>3<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Each pseudostem normally produces a single inflorescence, also known as the "banana heart". After fruiting, the pseudostem dies, but offshoots will normally have developed from the base, so that the plant as a whole is <a href="/wiki/Perennial" title="Perennial">perennial</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEStoverSimmonds1987244–247&#95;8-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStoverSimmonds1987244–247-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The inflorescence contains many petal-like <a href="/wiki/Bract" title="Bract">bracts</a> between rows of flowers. The female flowers (which can develop into fruit) appear in rows further up the stem (closer to the leaves) from the rows of male flowers. The ovary is <a href="/wiki/Ovary_(plants)" class="mw-redirect" title="Ovary (plants)">inferior</a>, meaning that the tiny petals and other flower parts appear at the tip of the ovary.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEOffice&#95;of&#95;the&#95;Gene&#95;Technology&#95;Regulator2008&#95;9-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOffice_of_the_Gene_Technology_Regulator2008-9"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>9<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The banana fruits develop from the <i>banana heart</i>, in a large hanging cluster called a <i>bunch</i>, made up of around nine tiers called <i>hands</i>, with up to 20 fruits to a hand. A bunch can weigh 22–65 kilograms (49–143&#160;lb).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-10" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-10"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>10<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The stalk ends of the fruits connect up to the <a href="/wiki/Rachis" title="Rachis">rachis</a> part of the inflorescence. Opposite the stalk end, is the <a href="/wiki/Blossom" title="Blossom">blossom</a> end, where the remnants of the flower deviate the texture from the rest of the flesh inside the peel.
</p><p>The fruit has been described as a "leathery berry".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Smith-1977&#95;11-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Smith-1977-11"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>11<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> There is a protective outer layer (a peel or skin) with numerous long, thin strings (<a href="/wiki/Vascular_bundle" title="Vascular bundle">Vascular bundles</a>), which run lengthwise between the skin and the edible inner white flesh. The peel is less palatable and usually discarded after peeling the fruit. The inner part of the common yellow dessert variety can be split lengthwise into three sections that correspond to the inner portions of the three <a href="/wiki/Carpel" class="mw-redirect" title="Carpel">carpels</a> by manually deforming the unopened fruit.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Warkentin-2004&#95;12-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Warkentin-2004-12"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>12<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In cultivated varieties, fertile seeds are usually absent.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Simmonds-1962&#95;13-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Simmonds-1962-13"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>13<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-14" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-14"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>14<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>

<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Evolution">Evolution</h2></div>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Phylogeny">Phylogeny</h3></div>
<p>A 2011 phylogenomic analysis using nuclear genes indicates the <a href="/wiki/Phylogeny" class="mw-redirect" title="Phylogeny">phylogeny</a> of some representatives of the <a href="/wiki/Musaceae" title="Musaceae">Musaceae</a> family. Major edible kinds of banana are shown in <b>boldface</b>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Christelová-2011&#95;15-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Christelová-2011-15"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>15<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>

<p><i><a href="/wiki/Musella_lasiocarpa" title="Musella lasiocarpa">Musella lasiocarpa</a></i>, Golden lotus banana of China
</p>
</td></tr>

<dl><dd><sup>‡</sup> <small>Many <a href="/wiki/List_of_banana_cultivars" title="List of banana cultivars">cultivated bananas</a> are hybrids of <i>M. acuminata</i> x <i>M. balbisiana</i> (not shown in tree).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-hybrid&#95;16-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hybrid-16"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>16<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></small></dd></dl>
<p>Work by Li and colleagues in 2024 identifies three subspecies of <i>M.&#160;acuminata</i>, namely sspp. <i>banksii</i>, <i>malaccensis</i>, and <i>zebrina</i>, as contributing substantially to the <i>Ban</i>, <i>Dh</i>, and <i>Ze</i> subgenomes of triploid cultivated bananas respectively.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Li-2024&#95;17-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Li-2024-17"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>17<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Taxonomy">Taxonomy</h3></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/List_of_banana_cultivars" title="List of banana cultivars">List of banana cultivars</a></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-right" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Nedravazhakola.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Nedravazhakola.jpg/250px-Nedravazhakola.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="188" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2d/Nedravazhakola.jpg/500px-Nedravazhakola.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="2048" data-file-height="1536" /></a><figcaption><i>Musa</i> 'Nendran' <a href="/wiki/Cultivar" title="Cultivar">cultivar</a>, grown widely in the Indian state of <a href="/wiki/Kerala" title="Kerala">Kerala</a> </figcaption></figure>
<p>The genus <i><a href="/wiki/Musa_(genus)" title="Musa (genus)">Musa</a></i> was created by <a href="/wiki/Carl_Linnaeus" title="Carl Linnaeus">Carl Linnaeus</a> in 1753.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-POWO&#95;18-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-POWO-18"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>18<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The name may be derived from <a href="/wiki/Antonius_Musa" title="Antonius Musa">Antonius Musa</a>, physician to the Emperor <a href="/wiki/Augustus" title="Augustus">Augustus</a>, or Linnaeus may have adapted the Arabic word for banana, <i><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/%D9%85%D9%88%D8%B2" class="extiw" title="wikt:موز">mauz</a></i>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Hyam-1995&#95;19-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hyam-1995-19"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>19<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The ultimate origin of <i>musa</i> may be in the <a href="/wiki/Trans%E2%80%93New_Guinea_languages" title="Trans–New Guinea languages">Trans–New Guinea languages</a>, which have words similar to "#muku"; from there the name was borrowed into the <a href="/wiki/Austronesian_languages" title="Austronesian languages">Austronesian languages</a> and across Asia, accompanying the cultivation of the banana as it was brought to new areas, via the <a href="/wiki/Dravidian_languages" title="Dravidian languages">Dravidian languages</a> of India, into Arabic as a <i><a href="/wiki/Wanderwort" title="Wanderwort">Wanderwort</a></i>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-20" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-20"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>20<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The word "banana" is thought to be of West African origin, possibly from the <a href="/wiki/Wolof_language" title="Wolof language">Wolof</a> word <span title="Wolof-language text"><i lang="wo"><a href="https://en.wiktionary.org/wiki/banaana" class="extiw" title="wikt:banaana">banaana</a></i></span>, and passed into English via Spanish or Portuguese.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-OnlineEtymologyDictionary&#95;21-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-OnlineEtymologyDictionary-21"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>21<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p><i>Musa</i> is the type genus in the family <a href="/wiki/Musaceae" title="Musaceae">Musaceae</a>. The <a href="/wiki/APG_III_system" title="APG III system">APG III system</a> assigns Musaceae to the order <a href="/wiki/Zingiberales" title="Zingiberales">Zingiberales</a>, part of the <a href="/wiki/Commelinid" class="mw-redirect" title="Commelinid">commelinid</a> clade of the <a href="/wiki/Monocotyledon" title="Monocotyledon">monocotyledonous</a> flowering plants. Some 85 species of <i>Musa</i> were recognized by <a href="/wiki/Plants_of_the_World_Online" title="Plants of the World Online">Plants of the World Online</a> as of July&#160;2025<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Banana&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>;<sup id="cite&#95;ref-POWO&#95;18-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-POWO-18"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>18<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> several produce edible fruit, while others are cultivated as ornamentals.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Bailey-1916&#95;22-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bailey-1916-22"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>22<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The classification of cultivated bananas has long been a problematic issue for taxonomists. Linnaeus originally placed bananas into two species based only on their uses as food: <i>Musa sapientum</i> for dessert bananas and <i>Musa paradisiaca</i> for <a href="/wiki/Plantain_(true)" class="mw-redirect" title="Plantain (true)">plantains</a>. More species names were added, but this approach proved to be inadequate for the number of <a href="/wiki/Cultivar" title="Cultivar">cultivars</a> in the primary <a href="/wiki/Center_of_diversity" class="mw-redirect" title="Center of diversity">center of diversity</a> of the genus, Southeast Asia. Many of these cultivars were given names that were later discovered to be <a href="/wiki/Synonym_(taxonomy)" title="Synonym (taxonomy)">synonyms</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEValmayorJamaluddinSilayoiKusumo2000&#95;23-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEValmayorJamaluddinSilayoiKusumo2000-23"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>23<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In a series of papers published from 1947 onward, Ernest Cheesman showed that Linnaeus's <i>Musa sapientum</i> and <i>Musa paradisiaca</i> were cultivars and descendants of two wild seed-producing species, <i><a href="/wiki/Musa_acuminata" title="Musa acuminata">Musa acuminata</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Musa_balbisiana" title="Musa balbisiana">Musa balbisiana</a></i>, both first described by <a href="/wiki/Luigi_Aloysius_Colla" title="Luigi Aloysius Colla">Luigi Aloysius Colla</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Stover-1987&#95;24-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stover-1987-24"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>24<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Cheesman recommended the abolition of Linnaeus's species in favor of reclassifying bananas according to three morphologically distinct groups of cultivars – those primarily exhibiting the botanical characteristics of <i>Musa balbisiana</i>, those primarily exhibiting the botanical characteristics of <i>Musa acuminata</i>, and those with characteristics of both.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEValmayorJamaluddinSilayoiKusumo2000&#95;23-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEValmayorJamaluddinSilayoiKusumo2000-23"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>23<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Researchers Norman Simmonds and Ken Shepherd proposed a genome-based nomenclature system in 1955. This system eliminated almost all the difficulties and inconsistencies of the earlier classification of bananas based on assigning scientific names to cultivated varieties. Despite this, the original names are still recognized by some authorities, leading to confusion.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Stover-1987&#95;24-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Stover-1987-24"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>24<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Porcher-2002&#95;25-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Porcher-2002-25"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>25<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The accepted <a href="/wiki/Botanical_name" title="Botanical name">scientific names</a> for most groups of cultivated bananas are <i>Musa acuminata</i> <small>Colla</small> and <i>Musa balbisiana</i> <small>Colla</small> for the ancestral species, and <a href="/wiki/Musa_%C3%97_paradisiaca" title="Musa × paradisiaca"><i>Musa</i> × <i>paradisiaca</i></a> <small>L.</small> for the hybrid of the two.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-hybrid&#95;16-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-hybrid-16"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>16<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>An unusual feature of the genetics of the banana is that <a href="/wiki/Chloroplast_DNA" class="mw-redirect" title="Chloroplast DNA">chloroplast DNA</a> is inherited maternally, while <a href="/wiki/Mitochondrial_DNA" title="Mitochondrial DNA">mitochondrial DNA</a> is inherited paternally. This facilitates taxonomic study of species and subspecies relationships.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Donohue-2010&#95;26-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Donohue-2010-26"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>26<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Informal_classification">Informal classification</h3></div>
<p>In regions such as North America and Europe, <i>Musa</i> fruits offered for sale can be divided into small sweet "bananas" eaten raw when ripe as a dessert, and large starchy "plantains" or <a href="/wiki/Cooking_banana" title="Cooking banana">cooking bananas</a>, which do not have to be ripe. Linnaeus made this distinction when naming two "species" of <i>Musa</i>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEValmayorJamaluddinSilayoiKusumo20002&#95;27-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEValmayorJamaluddinSilayoiKusumo20002-27"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>27<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Members of the "<a href="/wiki/Plantain_(true)" class="mw-redirect" title="Plantain (true)">plantain subgroup</a>" of banana cultivars, most important as food in West Africa and Latin America, correspond to this description, having long pointed fruit. They are described by Ploetz et al. as "true" plantains, distinct from other cooking bananas.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEPloetzKeplerDaniellsNelson200718–19&#95;28-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPloetzKeplerDaniellsNelson200718–19-28"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>28<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The cooking bananas of East Africa belong to a different group, the <a href="/wiki/East_African_Highland_banana" class="mw-redirect" title="East African Highland banana">East African Highland bananas</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEPloetzKeplerDaniellsNelson200712&#95;7-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPloetzKeplerDaniellsNelson200712-7"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>7<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Further, small farmers in Colombia grow a much wider range of cultivars than large commercial plantations do,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Gibert-2009&#95;29-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gibert-2009-29"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>29<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and in Southeast Asia—the center of diversity for bananas, both wild and cultivated—the distinction between "bananas" and "plantains" does not work. Many bananas are used both raw and cooked. There are starchy cooking bananas which are smaller than those eaten raw. The range of colors, sizes and shapes is far wider than in those grown or sold in Africa, Europe or the Americas.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEValmayorJamaluddinSilayoiKusumo20002&#95;27-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEValmayorJamaluddinSilayoiKusumo20002-27"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>27<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Southeast Asian languages do not make the distinction between "bananas" and "plantains" that is made in English. Thus both Cavendish dessert bananas and <a href="/wiki/Saba_banana" title="Saba banana">Saba cooking bananas</a> are called <i>pisang</i> in Malaysia and Indonesia, <i>kluai</i> in Thailand and <i>chuối</i> in Vietnam.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEValmayorJamaluddinSilayoiKusumo20008–12&#95;30-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEValmayorJamaluddinSilayoiKusumo20008–12-30"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>30<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Fe%27i_banana" title="Fe&#39;i banana">Fe'i bananas</a>, grown and eaten in the islands of the Pacific, are derived from a different wild species. Most Fe'i bananas are cooked, but <a href="/wiki/Karat_banana" title="Karat banana">Karat bananas</a>, which are short and squat with bright red skins, are eaten raw.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Englberger-2003&#95;31-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Englberger-2003-31"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>31<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="History">History</h2></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Banana_ancestors_(Musa_acuminata_and_Musa_balbisiana)_original_range.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Banana_ancestors_%28Musa_acuminata_and_Musa_balbisiana%29_original_range.svg/250px-Banana_ancestors_%28Musa_acuminata_and_Musa_balbisiana%29_original_range.svg.png" decoding="async" width="250" height="145" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/98/Banana_ancestors_%28Musa_acuminata_and_Musa_balbisiana%29_original_range.svg/500px-Banana_ancestors_%28Musa_acuminata_and_Musa_balbisiana%29_original_range.svg.png 1.5x" data-file-width="689" data-file-height="400" /></a><figcaption>Original <a href="/wiki/Range_(biology)" class="mw-redirect" title="Range (biology)">native ranges</a> of the ancestors of modern edible bananas.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-De&#95;Langhe&#95;2009&#95;32-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-De_Langhe_2009-32"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>32<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r981673959">.mw-parser-output .legend{page-break-inside:avoid;break-inside:avoid-column}.mw-parser-output .legend-color{display:inline-block;min-width:1.25em;height:1.25em;line-height:1.25;margin:1px 0;text-align:center;border:1px solid black;background-color:transparent;color:black}.mw-parser-output .legend-text{}</style><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#007800; color:white;-webkit-print-color-adjust: exact; print-color-adjust: exact;">&#160;</span>&#160;<i><a href="/wiki/Musa_acuminata" title="Musa acuminata">Musa acuminata</a></i></div> <link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r981673959" /><div class="legend"><span class="legend-color mw-no-invert" style="background-color:#ff9b35; color:black;-webkit-print-color-adjust: exact; print-color-adjust: exact;">&#160;</span>&#160;<i><a href="/wiki/Musa_balbisiana" title="Musa balbisiana">Musa balbisiana</a></i></div> </figcaption></figure>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Domestication">Domestication</h3></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/Musa_acuminata" title="Musa acuminata">Musa acuminata</a>, <a href="/wiki/Domesticated_plants_and_animals_of_Austronesia" title="Domesticated plants and animals of Austronesia">Domesticated plants and animals of Austronesia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/East_African_Highland_bananas" class="mw-redirect" title="East African Highland bananas">East African Highland bananas</a></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Inside_a_wild-type_banana.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="Photo of two cross-sectional halves of seed-filled fruit." src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Inside_a_wild-type_banana.jpg/250px-Inside_a_wild-type_banana.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="188" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/eb/Inside_a_wild-type_banana.jpg/500px-Inside_a_wild-type_banana.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="1360" data-file-height="1024" /></a><figcaption>Fruits of <a href="/wiki/Wild_type" title="Wild type">wild-type</a> bananas have numerous large, hard seeds.</figcaption></figure>
<p>The earliest domestication of bananas (<i><a href="/wiki/Musa_(genus)" title="Musa (genus)">Musa</a></i> spp.) was from naturally occurring <a href="/wiki/Parthenocarpic" class="mw-redirect" title="Parthenocarpic">parthenocarpic</a> (seedless) individuals of <i><a href="/wiki/Musa_banksii" title="Musa banksii">Musa banksii</a></i> in <a href="/wiki/New_Guinea" title="New Guinea">New Guinea</a>. These were cultivated by <a href="/wiki/Indigenous_people_of_New_Guinea" title="Indigenous people of New Guinea">Papuans</a> before the arrival of <a href="/wiki/Austronesian_peoples" title="Austronesian peoples">Austronesian-speakers</a>. Numerous <a href="/wiki/Phytolith" title="Phytolith">phytoliths</a> of bananas have been recovered from the <a href="/wiki/Kuk_Swamp" title="Kuk Swamp">Kuk Swamp</a> archaeological site and dated to around 10,000 to 6,500 <a href="/wiki/Before_Present" title="Before Present">BP</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Denham2011&#95;33-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Denham2011-33"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>33<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Perrier2009&#95;34-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Perrier2009-34"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>34<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Fuller2015&#95;35-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fuller2015-35"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>35<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Foraging" title="Foraging">Foraging</a> humans in this area began domestication in the late <a href="/wiki/Pleistocene" title="Pleistocene">Pleistocene</a> using <a href="/wiki/Transplanting" title="Transplanting">transplantation</a> and early <a href="/wiki/Tillage" title="Tillage">cultivation</a> methods.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Roberts-et-al-2017&#95;36-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Roberts-et-al-2017-36"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>36<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Harris-Hillman-1989&#95;37-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Harris-Hillman-1989-37"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>37<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Various investigations<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Harris-Hillman-1989&#95;37-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Harris-Hillman-1989-37"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>37<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Roberts-et-al-2017&#95;36-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Roberts-et-al-2017-36"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>36<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> –  including Denham <i>et al.</i>, 2003 – determine that by the early to middle of the <a href="/wiki/Holocene" title="Holocene">Holocene</a> the process was complete.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Roberts-et-al-2017&#95;36-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Roberts-et-al-2017-36"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>36<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Ancient_spread">Ancient spread</h3></div>
<p>From New Guinea, cultivated bananas spread westward into <a href="/wiki/Island_Southeast_Asia" class="mw-redirect" title="Island Southeast Asia">Island Southeast Asia</a>. They <a href="/wiki/Hybrid_(biology)" title="Hybrid (biology)">hybridized</a> with other (possibly independently domesticated) <a href="/wiki/Subspecies" title="Subspecies">subspecies</a> of <i><a href="/wiki/Musa_acuminata" title="Musa acuminata">Musa acuminata</a></i> as well as <i><a href="/wiki/Musa_balbisiana" title="Musa balbisiana">M. balbisiana</a></i> in the Philippines, northern New Guinea, and possibly <a href="/wiki/Halmahera" title="Halmahera">Halmahera</a>. These hybridization events produced the triploid <a href="/wiki/List_of_banana_cultivars" title="List of banana cultivars">cultivars of bananas</a> commonly grown today. From Island Southeast Asia, they became part of the <a href="/wiki/Domesticated_plants_and_animals_of_Austronesia" title="Domesticated plants and animals of Austronesia">staple domesticated crops</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Austronesian_peoples" title="Austronesian peoples">Austronesian peoples</a> and were spread via their <a href="/wiki/Austronesian_expansion" class="mw-redirect" title="Austronesian expansion">ancient seaborne migrations</a> and <a href="/wiki/Austronesian_maritime_trade_network" class="mw-redirect" title="Austronesian maritime trade network">ancient maritime trading routes</a> into Oceania, <a href="/wiki/Africa" title="Africa">Africa</a>, <a href="/wiki/South_Asia" title="South Asia">South Asia</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Indochina" class="mw-redirect" title="Indochina">Indochina</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Perrier&#95;2019&#95;38-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Perrier_2019-38"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>38<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Chronological_dispersal_of_Austronesian_people_across_the_Pacific_(per_Benton_et_al,_2012,_adapted_from_Bellwood,_2011).png" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Chronological_dispersal_of_Austronesian_people_across_the_Pacific_%28per_Benton_et_al%2C_2012%2C_adapted_from_Bellwood%2C_2011%29.png/250px-Chronological_dispersal_of_Austronesian_people_across_the_Pacific_%28per_Benton_et_al%2C_2012%2C_adapted_from_Bellwood%2C_2011%29.png" decoding="async" width="250" height="105" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e4/Chronological_dispersal_of_Austronesian_people_across_the_Pacific_%28per_Benton_et_al%2C_2012%2C_adapted_from_Bellwood%2C_2011%29.png/500px-Chronological_dispersal_of_Austronesian_people_across_the_Pacific_%28per_Benton_et_al%2C_2012%2C_adapted_from_Bellwood%2C_2011%29.png 1.5x" data-file-width="7268" data-file-height="3065" /></a><figcaption><a href="/wiki/Austronesian_expansion" class="mw-redirect" title="Austronesian expansion">Chronological dispersal</a> of <a href="/wiki/Austronesian_peoples" title="Austronesian peoples">Austronesian peoples</a> across the <a href="/wiki/Indo-Pacific" title="Indo-Pacific">Indo-Pacific</a><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Chambers-2013&#95;39-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chambers-2013-39"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>39<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></figcaption></figure>
<p>Bananas are believed to have been introduced to Africa from Southeast Asia via the Austronesian settlement of <a href="/wiki/Madagascar" title="Madagascar">Madagascar</a> (<abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;">&#8201;600 AD</span>).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Power&#95;40-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Power-40"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>40<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Beaujard2011&#95;41-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Beaujard2011-41"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>41<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Anderson2019&#95;42-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Anderson2019-42"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>42<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This is supported by <a href="/wiki/Relict_(biology)" title="Relict (biology)">relict populations</a> of (accidentally-introduced) seeded <i><a href="/wiki/Musa_acuminata" title="Musa acuminata">Musa acuminata</a></i> in northeastern Madagasacar and <a href="/wiki/Pemba_Island" title="Pemba Island">Pemba Island</a> (off <a href="/wiki/Tanzania" title="Tanzania">Tanzania</a>), as well as the phenotypes of cultivated polyploid bananas, and their distribution; all of which provide the clearest evidence of a Southeast Asian origin.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Power&#95;40-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Power-40"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>40<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Beaujard2011&#95;41-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Beaujard2011-41"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>41<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> However, the role of Madagascar as a staging board for the dispersal of bananas (and other Asian crops) is unclear. <a href="/wiki/Malagasy_people" class="mw-redirect" title="Malagasy people">Malagasy people</a> colonized Madagascar from Island Southeast Asia around 600 AD onwards, but contact between East Africa and Island Southeast Asia dates back to at least 300 BC or earlier. The possibility that bananas may have been introduced from earlier Austronesian settlements in the East African coast that pre-date the settlement of Madagascar can not be ruled out.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-43" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-43"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>43<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>These ancient introductions resulted in the banana subgroup now known as the <a href="/wiki/True_plantains" title="True plantains">"true" plantains</a>, which include the <a href="/wiki/East_African_Highland_bananas" class="mw-redirect" title="East African Highland bananas">East African Highland bananas</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Pacific_plantains" class="mw-redirect" title="Pacific plantains">Pacific plantains</a> (the <a href="/wiki/Iholena_bananas" class="mw-redirect" title="Iholena bananas">Iholena</a> and <a href="/wiki/Maoli-Popo%27ulu_bananas" class="mw-redirect" title="Maoli-Popo&#39;ulu bananas">Maoli-Popo'ulu</a> subgroups). Genetic evidence show that East African Highland bananas (AAA) originated from banana populations introduced to Africa from the region between <a href="/wiki/Java" title="Java">Java</a>, <a href="/wiki/Borneo" title="Borneo">Borneo</a>, and <a href="/wiki/New_Guinea" title="New Guinea">New Guinea</a>. Pacific plantains (AAB), on the other hand, were introduced to the Pacific Islands from banana populations originating from either eastern New Guinea or the <a href="/wiki/Bismarck_Archipelago" title="Bismarck Archipelago">Bismarck Archipelago</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Denham2011&#95;33-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Denham2011-33"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>33<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Perrier2009&#95;34-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Perrier2009-34"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>34<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Another wave of introductions later spread domesticated polyploid bananas to other parts of <a href="/wiki/Tropical_Asia" title="Tropical Asia">tropical Asia</a>, particularly <a href="/wiki/Indochina" class="mw-redirect" title="Indochina">Indochina</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Indian_subcontinent" title="Indian subcontinent">Indian subcontinent</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Denham2011&#95;33-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Denham2011-33"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>33<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Perrier2009&#95;34-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Perrier2009-34"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>34<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Southeast Asia remains the region of <a href="/wiki/Center_of_diversity" class="mw-redirect" title="Center of diversity">primary diversity</a> of the banana. Areas of secondary diversity are found in Africa, indicating a long history of banana cultivation there.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEPloetzKeplerDaniellsNelson20077&#95;44-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPloetzKeplerDaniellsNelson20077-44"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>44<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Other_hypotheses">Other hypotheses</h4></div>
<p>21st century discoveries of alleged banana <a href="/wiki/Phytolith" title="Phytolith">phytoliths</a> in <a href="/wiki/Uganda" title="Uganda">Uganda</a> and <a href="/wiki/Cameroon" title="Cameroon">Cameroon</a> dating to the first millennium BC and earlier triggered a debate about the date of the first introduction of bananas to <a href="/wiki/East_Africa" title="East Africa">East Africa</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Power&#95;40-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Power-40"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>40<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Mbida-2000&#95;45-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mbida-2000-45"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>45<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Lejju-2005&#95;46-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lejju-2005-46"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>46<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>However, the identification of the remains in Uganda as phytoliths, much less banana phytoliths, is now considered dubious. The Cameroon phytoliths, on the other hand, are confirmed as <i>Musa</i>, despite early doubts that they may be from <i><a href="/wiki/Ensete" title="Ensete">Ensete</a></i>. However, the incongruous early date (all other <a href="/wiki/Archaeobotanical" class="mw-redirect" title="Archaeobotanical">archaeobotanical</a> remains of bananas in Africa being from at earliest the first millennium AD) remains questionable due to the low number of phytoliths recovered (25), the absence of additional phytoliths in more recent sediments, and the possibility that the apparent date was the result of <a href="/wiki/Stratigraphy_(archaeology)" title="Stratigraphy (archaeology)">stratigraphic</a> mixing.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Power&#95;40-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Power-40"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>40<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> An introduction date of 2000 to 1000 BC is also unlikely as this was long before there were any evidence of <a href="/wiki/History_of_agriculture" title="History of agriculture">agriculture</a> in East Africa. <a href="/wiki/Polyploid" class="mw-redirect" title="Polyploid">Polyploid</a> banana cultivars are sterile and do not spread without human cultivation.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Power&#95;40-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Power-40"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>40<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Similarly, phytoliths recovered from the <a href="/wiki/Kot_Diji" title="Kot Diji">Kot Diji</a> archaeological site in <a href="/wiki/Pakistan" title="Pakistan">Pakistan</a> were interpreted as evidence that bananas were known to the <a href="/wiki/Indus_Valley_civilisation" class="mw-redirect" title="Indus Valley civilisation">Indus Valley civilisation</a>. This may indicate very early dispersal of bananas by Austronesian traders by sea from as early as 2000 BCE. But this is still putative, as they may have come from local wild <i>Musa</i> species used for fiber or as ornamentals, not food; and banana phytoliths are absent in other contemporary sites in <a href="/wiki/South_Asia" title="South Asia">South Asia</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Fuller2015&#95;35-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Fuller2015-35"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>35<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Glucanase" title="Glucanase">Glucanase</a> and two other proteins specific to <a href="/wiki/Musaceae" title="Musaceae">Musaceae</a> were found in <a href="/wiki/Dental_calculus" class="mw-redirect" title="Dental calculus">dental calculus</a> from the early <a href="/wiki/Iron_Age" title="Iron Age">Iron Age</a> (12th century BCE) <a href="/wiki/Philistines" title="Philistines">Philistines</a> in <a href="/wiki/Tel_Erani" title="Tel Erani">Tel Erani</a> in the southern <a href="/wiki/Levant" title="Levant">Levant</a>. However, the authors only tentatively identify it as <i>Musa</i>, as the proteins can also be found in <i><a href="/wiki/Ensete" title="Ensete">Ensete</a></i> (cultivated for their edible corms and pseudostems in Africa).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-47" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-47"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>47<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Arab_Agricultural_Revolution">Arab Agricultural Revolution</h3></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Arab_Agricultural_Revolution" title="Arab Agricultural Revolution">Arab Agricultural Revolution</a></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Bananas_Muslim_world.JPG" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="Map stating that banana cultivation occurred in pre-Islamic times in India and Southeast Asia, during the 700–1500 CE &quot;Islamic period&quot; along the Nile River and in Mesopotamia and Palestine, and less-certainly in sub-Saharan Africa during that same period" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Bananas_Muslim_world.JPG/250px-Bananas_Muslim_world.JPG" decoding="async" width="250" height="135" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e7/Bananas_Muslim_world.JPG/500px-Bananas_Muslim_world.JPG 1.5x" data-file-width="535" data-file-height="289" /></a><figcaption>Actual and probable diffusion of bananas during the <a href="/wiki/Arab_Agricultural_Revolution" title="Arab Agricultural Revolution">Arab Agricultural Revolution</a> (700–1500 CE)<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Watson-1983&#95;48-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Watson-1983-48"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>48<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></figcaption></figure>
<p>The banana may have been present in isolated locations in the Middle East on the eve of <a href="/wiki/Islam" title="Islam">Islam</a>. The <a href="/wiki/Spread_of_Islam" title="Spread of Islam">spread of Islam</a> was followed by far-reaching diffusion. There are numerous references to it in Islamic texts (such as poems and <a href="/wiki/Hadith" title="Hadith">hadiths</a>) from the 9th century onwards. By the 10th century, the banana appeared in texts from <a href="/wiki/Palestine_(region)" title="Palestine (region)">Palestine</a> and Egypt. From there, it diffused into North Africa and <a href="/wiki/Al-Andalus" title="Al-Andalus">Al-Andalus</a> (Islamic Spain) during the <a href="/wiki/Arab_Agricultural_Revolution" title="Arab Agricultural Revolution">Arab Agricultural Revolution</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Watson-1974&#95;49-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Watson-1974-49"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>49<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Watson-1983&#95;48-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Watson-1983-48"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>48<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> An article on banana tree cultivation is included in <a href="/wiki/Ibn_al-%27Awwam" title="Ibn al-&#39;Awwam">Ibn al-'Awwam</a>'s 12th-century agricultural work, <i>Kitāb al-Filāḥa</i> (<i>Book on Agriculture</i>).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-50" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-50"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>50<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> During the Middle Ages, bananas from <a href="/wiki/Granada" title="Granada">Granada</a> were considered among the best in the Arab world.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Watson-1983&#95;48-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Watson-1983-48"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>48<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Bananas were certainly grown in the Christian <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Cyprus" title="Kingdom of Cyprus">Kingdom of Cyprus</a> by the late medieval period. Writing in 1458, the Italian traveller and writer <a href="/wiki/Gabriele_Capodilista" title="Gabriele Capodilista">Gabriele Capodilista</a> wrote favourably of the extensive farm produce of the estates at Episkopi, near modern-day <a href="/wiki/Limassol" title="Limassol">Limassol</a>, including the area's banana plantations.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Jennings-1992&#95;51-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jennings-1992-51"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>51<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Early_modern_spread">Early modern spread</h3></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Columbian_exchange" title="Columbian exchange">Columbian exchange</a></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Acta_Eruditorum_-_III_musa_arabum_pala_plinii,_1734_%E2%80%93_BEIC_13446956.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Acta_Eruditorum_-_III_musa_arabum_pala_plinii%2C_1734_%E2%80%93_BEIC_13446956.jpg/250px-Acta_Eruditorum_-_III_musa_arabum_pala_plinii%2C_1734_%E2%80%93_BEIC_13446956.jpg" decoding="async" width="190" height="276" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Acta_Eruditorum_-_III_musa_arabum_pala_plinii%2C_1734_%E2%80%93_BEIC_13446956.jpg/330px-Acta_Eruditorum_-_III_musa_arabum_pala_plinii%2C_1734_%E2%80%93_BEIC_13446956.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Acta_Eruditorum_-_III_musa_arabum_pala_plinii%2C_1734_%E2%80%93_BEIC_13446956.jpg/500px-Acta_Eruditorum_-_III_musa_arabum_pala_plinii%2C_1734_%E2%80%93_BEIC_13446956.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1722" data-file-height="2502" /></a><figcaption>Illustration of fruit and plant,<br /><i><a href="/wiki/Acta_Eruditorum" title="Acta Eruditorum">Acta Eruditorum</a></i>, 1734</figcaption></figure>
<p>In the <a href="/wiki/Early_modern_period" title="Early modern period">early modern period</a>, bananas were encountered by European explorers during the <a href="/wiki/Magellan_expedition" title="Magellan expedition">Magellan expedition</a> in 1521, in both <a href="/wiki/Guam" title="Guam">Guam</a> and the <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">Philippines</a>. Lacking a name for the fruit, the ship's historian <a href="/wiki/Antonio_Pigafetta" title="Antonio Pigafetta">Antonio Pigafetta</a> described them as "figs more than one <a href="/wiki/Palm_(unit)" title="Palm (unit)">palm</a> long."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-52" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-52"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>52<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Nowell-1962&#95;53-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nowell-1962-53"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>53<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup class="reference nowrap"><span title="Page / location: 130, 132">&#58;&#8202;130,&#8202;132&#8202;</span></sup> Bananas were introduced to <a href="/wiki/South_America" title="South America">South America</a> by Portuguese sailors who brought them from West Africa in the 16th century.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Gibson-2012&#95;54-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gibson-2012-54"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>54<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Southeast Asian banana cultivars, as well as <a href="/wiki/Abaca" class="mw-redirect" title="Abaca">abaca</a> grown for fibers, were introduced to North and Central America by the Spanish from the Philippines, via the <a href="/wiki/Manila_galleons" class="mw-redirect" title="Manila galleons">Manila galleons</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-55" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-55"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>55<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Plantation_cultivation">Plantation cultivation</h3></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/History_of_modern_banana_plantations_in_the_Americas" title="History of modern banana plantations in the Americas">History of modern banana plantations in the Americas</a></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Banana_Plantation_Panabo_City.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Banana_Plantation_Panabo_City.jpg/250px-Banana_Plantation_Panabo_City.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="188" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Banana_Plantation_Panabo_City.jpg/500px-Banana_Plantation_Panabo_City.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="1280" data-file-height="960" /></a><figcaption>Plantation in the Philippines, 2010</figcaption></figure>
<p>In the 15th and 16th centuries, Portuguese colonists started banana plantations in the Atlantic Islands, Brazil, and western Africa.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Phora-sotoby.com&#95;56-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Phora-sotoby.com-56"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>56<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> North Americans began consuming bananas on a small scale at very high prices shortly after the Civil War, though it was only in the 1880s that the food became more widespread.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Koeppel-2008&#95;57-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Koeppel-2008-57"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>57<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> As late as the <a href="/wiki/Victorian_Era" class="mw-redirect" title="Victorian Era">Victorian Era</a>, bananas were not widely known in Europe, although they were available.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Phora-sotoby.com&#95;56-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Phora-sotoby.com-56"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>56<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The earliest modern plantations originated in Jamaica and the related <a href="/wiki/Western_Caribbean_Zone" class="mw-redirect" title="Western Caribbean Zone">Western Caribbean Zone</a>, including most of <a href="/wiki/Central_America" title="Central America">Central America</a>. Plantation cultivation involved the combination of modern transportation networks of steamships and railroads with the development of refrigeration that allowed more time between harvesting and ripening. North American shippers like <a href="/wiki/Lorenzo_Dow_Baker" title="Lorenzo Dow Baker">Lorenzo Dow Baker</a> and <a href="/wiki/Andrew_Preston_(businessman)" title="Andrew Preston (businessman)">Andrew Preston</a>, the founders of the <a href="/wiki/Boston_Fruit_Company" title="Boston Fruit Company">Boston Fruit Company</a> started this process in the 1870s, with the participation of railroad builders like <a href="/wiki/Minor_C._Keith" class="mw-redirect" title="Minor C. Keith">Minor C. Keith</a>. Development led to the multi-national giant corporations like <a href="/wiki/Chiquita_Brands_International" class="mw-redirect" title="Chiquita Brands International">Chiquita</a> and <a href="/wiki/Dole_Food_Company" class="mw-redirect" title="Dole Food Company">Dole</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Koeppel-2008&#95;57-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Koeppel-2008-57"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>57<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> These companies were <a href="/wiki/Monopoly" title="Monopoly">monopolistic</a>, <a href="/wiki/Vertically_integrated" class="mw-redirect" title="Vertically integrated">vertically integrated</a> (controlling growing, processing, shipping and marketing) and usually used political manipulation to build <a href="/wiki/Enclave_economy" title="Enclave economy">enclave economies</a> (internally self-sufficient, virtually tax exempt, and export-oriented, contributing little to the host economy). Their political maneuvers, which gave rise to the term <a href="/wiki/Banana_republic" title="Banana republic">banana republic</a> for states such as Honduras and Guatemala, included working with local elites and their rivalries to influence politics or playing the international interests of the United States, especially during the <a href="/wiki/Cold_War" title="Cold War">Cold War</a>, to keep the political climate favorable to their interests.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-NZHerald-2008&#95;58-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NZHerald-2008-58"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>58<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Small-scale_cultivation">Small-scale cultivation</h3></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/History_of_peasant_banana_production_in_the_Americas" title="History of peasant banana production in the Americas">History of peasant banana production in the Americas</a></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Farm_Workers.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Farm_Workers.jpg/250px-Farm_Workers.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="141" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/75/Farm_Workers.jpg/500px-Farm_Workers.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="1696" data-file-height="954" /></a><figcaption>Small-scale banana production, Liberia, 2013</figcaption></figure>
<p>The vast majority of the world's bananas are cultivated for family consumption or for sale on local markets. They are grown in large quantities in India, while many other Asian and African countries host numerous small-scale banana growers who sell at least some of their crop.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEOffice&#95;of&#95;the&#95;Gene&#95;Technology&#95;Regulator20087–8&#95;59-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOffice_of_the_Gene_Technology_Regulator20087–8-59"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>59<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Peasants with smallholdings of 1 to 2 acres in the Caribbean produce bananas for the world market, often alongside other crops.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-60" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-60"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>60<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In many tropical countries, the main cultivars produce green (unripe) bananas used for <a href="/wiki/Cooking" title="Cooking">cooking</a>. Because bananas and plantains produce fruit year-round, they provide a valuable food source during the <i>hunger season</i> between harvests of other crops, and are thus important for global <a href="/wiki/Food_security" title="Food security">food security</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-d&#39;Hont-2012&#95;61-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-d&#39;Hont-2012-61"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>61<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Modern_cultivation">Modern cultivation</h2></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_banana_cultivars" title="List of banana cultivars">List of banana cultivars</a></div>
<p>Bananas are propagated <a href="/wiki/Asexual_reproduction" title="Asexual reproduction">asexually</a> from offshoots. The plant is allowed to produce two shoots at a time; a larger one for immediate fruiting and a smaller "sucker" or "follower" to produce fruit in 6–8 months.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FOOTNOTEStoverSimmonds1987244–247&#95;8-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEStoverSimmonds1987244–247-8"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>8<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> As a non-seasonal crop, bananas are available fresh year-round.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-62" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-62"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>62<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> They are grown in some 135 countries.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-63" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-63"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>63<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Cavendish">Cavendish</h3></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Cavendish_banana" title="Cavendish banana">Cavendish banana</a></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Bananas.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="Grocery store photo of several bunches of bananas" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Bananas.jpg/250px-Bananas.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="166" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4c/Bananas.jpg/500px-Bananas.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="3008" data-file-height="2000" /></a><figcaption>Cultivars in the <a href="/wiki/Cavendish_bananas" class="mw-redirect" title="Cavendish bananas">Cavendish</a> group dominate the world market.</figcaption></figure>
<p>In global commerce in 2009, by far the most important cultivars belonged to the triploid <i>Musa acuminata</i> <a href="/wiki/List_of_banana_cultivars#AAA_Group" title="List of banana cultivars">AAA group</a> of Cavendish group bananas.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-64" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-64"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>64<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Disease is threatening the production of the Cavendish banana worldwide. It is unclear if any existing cultivar can replace Cavendish bananas, so various <a href="/wiki/Hybrid_(biology)" title="Hybrid (biology)">hybridisation</a> and <a href="/wiki/Genetic_engineering" title="Genetic engineering">genetic engineering</a> programs are attempting to create a disease-resistant, mass-market banana. One such strain that has emerged is the Taiwanese Cavendish or Formosana.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Gittleson-2018&#95;65-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gittleson-2018-65"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>65<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Wang-2021&#95;66-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Wang-2021-66"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>66<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Canine-2005&#95;67-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Canine-2005-67"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>67<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Ripening">Ripening</h3></div>
<p>Export bananas are picked green, and ripened in special rooms upon arrival in the destination country. These rooms are air-tight and filled with <a href="/wiki/Ethylene_as_a_plant_hormone" class="mw-redirect" title="Ethylene as a plant hormone">ethylene gas</a> to induce ripening, which mimics the normal production of this gas as a ripening hormone.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FruitRipening&#95;68-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FruitRipening-68"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>68<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-ArgonneNationalLaboratory&#95;69-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ArgonneNationalLaboratory-69"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>69<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Ethylene stimulates the formation of <a href="/wiki/Amylase" title="Amylase">amylase</a>, an <a href="/wiki/Enzyme" title="Enzyme">enzyme</a> that breaks down starch into sugar, influencing the taste. Ethylene signals the production of <a href="/wiki/Pectinase" title="Pectinase">pectinase</a>, a different enzyme which breaks down the <a href="/wiki/Pectin" title="Pectin">pectin</a> between the cells of the banana, causing the banana to soften as it ripens.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FruitRipening&#95;68-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FruitRipening-68"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>68<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-ArgonneNationalLaboratory&#95;69-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ArgonneNationalLaboratory-69"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>69<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The vivid yellow color many consumers in temperate climates associate with bananas is caused by ripening around 18&#160;°C (64&#160;°F), and does not occur in Cavendish bananas ripened in tropical temperatures (over 27&#160;°C (81&#160;°F)), which leaves them green.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Ding-2007&#95;70-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ding-2007-70"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>70<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Sugianti-2024&#95;71-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sugianti-2024-71"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>71<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Storage_and_transport">Storage and transport</h3></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Overripe_banana_20211221_152406.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Overripe_banana_20211221_152406.jpg/250px-Overripe_banana_20211221_152406.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="132" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/48/Overripe_banana_20211221_152406.jpg/500px-Overripe_banana_20211221_152406.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="5344" data-file-height="2832" /></a><figcaption><i><a href="/wiki/Ralstonia_solanacearum" title="Ralstonia solanacearum">Ralstonia solanacearum</a></i> on an overripe banana</figcaption></figure>
<p>Bananas are transported over long distances from the tropics to world markets.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Arias-2003&#95;72-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Arias-2003-72"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>72<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> To obtain maximum shelf life, harvest comes before the fruit is mature. The fruit requires careful handling, rapid transport to ports, cooling, and refrigerated shipping. The goal is to prevent the bananas from producing their natural ripening agent, ethylene. This technology allows storage and transport for 3–4 weeks at 13&#160;°C (55&#160;°F). On arrival, bananas are held at about 17&#160;°C (63&#160;°F) and treated with a low concentration of ethylene. After a few days, the fruit begins to ripen and is distributed for final sale. Ripe bananas can be held for a few days at home. If bananas are too green, they can be put in a brown paper bag with an apple or tomato overnight to speed up the ripening process.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Chiquita-2009&#95;73-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Chiquita-2009-73"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>73<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Bebber-2023&#95;74-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bebber-2023-74"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>74<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Sustainability">Sustainability</h3></div>
<p>The excessive use of fertilizers contributes greatly to <a href="/wiki/Eutrophication" title="Eutrophication">eutrophication</a> in streams and lakes, harming aquatic life, while expanding banana production has led to deforestation. As soil nutrients are depleted, more forest is cleared for plantations. This causes soil erosion and increases the frequency of flooding.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Cohen-2009&#95;75-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Cohen-2009-75"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>75<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Sustainability_standards_and_certification" title="Sustainability standards and certification">Voluntary sustainability standards</a> such as <a href="/wiki/Rainforest_Alliance" title="Rainforest Alliance">Rainforest Alliance</a> and <a href="/wiki/Fairtrade_certification" class="mw-redirect" title="Fairtrade certification">Fairtrade</a> are being used to address some of these issues. Banana production certified in this way grew rapidly at the start of the 21st century to represent 36% of banana exports by 2016.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-76" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-76"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>76<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> However, such standards are applied mainly in countries which focus on the export market, such as Colombia, Costa Rica, Ecuador, and Guatemala; worldwide they cover only 8–10% of production.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Voora-2023&#95;77-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Voora-2023-77"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>77<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Breeding">Breeding</h2></div>
<p>Edible bananas are <a href="/wiki/Parthenocarpic" class="mw-redirect" title="Parthenocarpic">parthenocarpic</a> (seedless). This is important for their edibility, but the lack of seed production makes breeding difficult. Furthermore, cultivated bananas are typically triploid (less commonly diploid, and a few tetraploid). They are typically derived from the wild diploid species <i><a href="/wiki/Musa_acuminata" title="Musa acuminata">M.&#160;acuminata</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Musa_balbisiana" title="Musa balbisiana">M.&#160;balbisiana</a></i>, although some originate from <i>M.&#160;acuminata</i> only.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-jeridi&#95;78-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-jeridi-78"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>78<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Unpaired chromosomes in triploids, <a href="/wiki/Homoeologous" class="mw-redirect" title="Homoeologous">homoeologous</a> recombination between chromosomes from the two ancestral species, and failures to produce functional reproductive structures (due to selection for parthenocarpy) all combine to cause extremely low frequencies of successful seed production when attempting to breed bananas. Starting in the 1920s, "pedigree breeding" has been employed to try to generate desirable hybrids. Existing triploids are bred against diploids, sometimes wild with a desired characteristic, trying to create a tetraploid by combining a haploid gamete from the diploid parent with a (rare) unreduced triploid gamete from the other. The resulting tetraploid can then be bred against another diploid to produce triploid offspring. More recently, the "reproductive breeding" strategy treats diploids with <a href="/wiki/Colchicine" title="Colchicine">colchicine</a>  to produce tetraploid offspring, which can then be bred against a second diploid to produce triploids.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-79" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-79"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>79<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Mutation_breeding" title="Mutation breeding">Mutation breeding</a> can be used in this crop. <a href="/wiki/Aneuploidy" title="Aneuploidy">Aneuploidy</a> is a source of significant variation in <a href="/wiki/Allotriploid" class="mw-redirect" title="Allotriploid">allotriploid</a> varieties. For one example, it can be a source of <a href="/wiki/Panama_disease#Tropical_Race_4/TR4" title="Panama disease">TR4 resistance</a>. <a href="/wiki/Lab_protocol" class="mw-redirect" title="Lab protocol">Lab protocols</a> have been devised to screen for such aberrations and for possible resulting disease resistances.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Jankowicz-Cieslak-2022&#95;80-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jankowicz-Cieslak-2022-80"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>80<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Crop_wild_relative" title="Crop wild relative">Wild <i>Musa</i> spp.</a> provide useful resistance genetics, and are vital to breeding for TR4 resistance, as shown in <a href="/wiki/Introgressed" class="mw-redirect" title="Introgressed">introgressed</a> resistance from wild relatives.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Ismaila-2023&#95;81-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ismaila-2023-81"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>81<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The <a href="/wiki/Honduran_Foundation_for_Agricultural_Research" class="mw-redirect" title="Honduran Foundation for Agricultural Research">Honduran Foundation for Agricultural Research</a> has bred a seedless banana that is resistant to both Panama disease and black Sigatoka disease. The team made use of the fact that "seedless" varieties do rarely produce seeds; they obtained around 15 seeds from some 30,000 cultivated plants, pollinated by hand with pollen from wild Asian bananas.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-82" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-82"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>82<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Production">Production</h2></div>
