<td colspan="4"><small>Source: <a href="/wiki/FAOSTAT" class="mw-redirect" title="FAOSTAT">FAOSTAT</a> of the <a href="/wiki/United_Nations" title="United Nations">United Nations</a><sup id="cite&#95;ref-FAOSTAT-2022&#95;83-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FAOSTAT-2022-83"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>83<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Note: Some countries distinguish between bananas and plantains, but four of the top six producers do not, thus necessitating comparisons using the total for bananas and plantains combined.</small>
</td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>As of 2018<sup class="plainlinks noexcerpt noprint asof-tag update" style="display:none;"><a class="external text" href="https://en.wikipedia.org/w/index.php?title=Banana&amp;action=edit">&#91;update&#93;</a></sup>, bananas are exported in larger volume and to a larger value than any other fruit.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Gittleson-2018&#95;65-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Gittleson-2018-65"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>65<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In 2022, world production of bananas and plantains combined was 179 million tonnes, led by India and China with a combined total of 26% of global production. Other major producers were Uganda, Indonesia, the Philippines, Nigeria and Ecuador.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FAOSTAT-2022&#95;83-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FAOSTAT-2022-83"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>83<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> As reported for 2013, total world exports were 20 million tonnes of bananas and 859,000 tonnes of plantains.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FAOSTAT-2017&#95;84-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FAOSTAT-2017-84"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>84<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Ecuador and the Philippines were the leading exporters with 5.4 and 3.3 million tonnes, respectively, and the Dominican Republic was the leading exporter of plantains with 210,350 tonnes.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FAOSTAT-2017&#95;84-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FAOSTAT-2017-84"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>84<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Ecology">Ecology</h2></div>
<p>Banana production is affected by <a href="/wiki/Climate_change" title="Climate change">climate change</a> through temperature, <a href="/wiki/Moisture_stress" title="Moisture stress">water stress</a>, geography, light exposure, pests and disease, and <a href="/wiki/Carbon_dioxide_in_the_atmosphere_of_Earth" title="Carbon dioxide in the atmosphere of Earth">carbon dioxide</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Abdoussalami&#95;2023&#95;85-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Abdoussalami_2023-85"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>85<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Overall, climate change from 1961 to 2016 suggests that climate change has had a net benefit on banana yields due to rising temperatures, but that these benefits are tapering off<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Varma&#95;2019&#95;86-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Varma_2019-86"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>86<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>  due to increased water demand and the challenge of growing <a href="/wiki/Companion_planting" title="Companion planting">companion crops</a> like <a href="/wiki/Coffee" title="Coffee">coffee</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Abdoussalami&#95;2023&#95;85-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Abdoussalami_2023-85"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>85<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Climate change effects vary greatly by region; study investigating climate change risk across the multiple countries responsible for 86% of banana production found Brazil, Colombia, Nicaragua, Panama, Malaysia to be 'at-risk', where banana yields are predicted to decline in response to climate change, while African countries as well as Honduras and Ecuador are predicted to be advantaged by climate change.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Varma&#95;2019&#95;86-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Varma_2019-86"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>86<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Some major producing countries, notably India and China, are considered 'adaptable' as while they do show potential negative effects of climate change on yields, strong technology-yield trends may mitigate climate-driven yield declines.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-87" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-87"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>87<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Shifting bioclimatic zones in some areas, such as <a href="/wiki/Nepal" title="Nepal">Nepal</a>, mean that banana cultivation may increase in some areas as other crops, such as coffee, suffer the effects of climate change in the same region. One study indicated the amount of area suited to banana growing in Nepal will grow by 40% by 2050, with some case studies showing benefit from the agroforestry technique of coffee-banana intercropping.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Ranjitkar&#95;2016&#95;88-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ranjitkar_2016-88"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>88<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> As part of these adaptations, "market opportunities for smallholder farmers need to be properly addressed through <a href="/wiki/Cooperative" title="Cooperative">cooperatives</a> or other such local bodies which secure adequate prices for farmers" (p. 14)<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Ranjitkar&#95;2016&#95;88-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Ranjitkar_2016-88"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>88<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Some banana diseases have spread in area due to climate change – for example, <a href="/wiki/Black_sigatoka" title="Black sigatoka">Black Sigatoka</a> has spread from Honduras to Brazil, the Caribbean, and most recently, Florida.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Bebber&#95;2019&#95;89-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bebber_2019-89"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>89<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>  Banana growing in Latin America and the Caribbean are predicted to have an increased risk of infection due to increased spore germination and growth, and wetter crop <a href="/wiki/Canopy_(botany)" title="Canopy (botany)">canopies</a>. However, predictions for Brazil suggest that due to reduced humidity, areas favorable to Black Sigatoka may decrease.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-90" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-90"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>90<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Bananas are negatively affected by flooding brought on or made worse by climate change.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Abdoussalami&#95;2023&#95;85-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Abdoussalami_2023-85"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>85<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Even in areas where drying reduces disease risk, the investment in needed <a href="/wiki/Irrigation" title="Irrigation">irrigation</a> systems could pressure freshwater resources and thus, "the impact of future climate change on banana production from the perspective of management must consider both the disease and the host" (p. 6).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Bebber&#95;2019&#95;89-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bebber_2019-89"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>89<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Also, farmers are limited by resources in <a href="/wiki/Drought" title="Drought">drought</a> management activities such as weeding, mulching, irrigation costs, and extra labor (p. 18).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Abdoussalami&#95;2023&#95;85-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Abdoussalami_2023-85"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>85<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Methods for mitigating adverse environmental impacts on banana production include cultivating "climate-resistant cultivars, <a href="/wiki/Integrated_pest_management" title="Integrated pest management">integrated pest management</a>, <a href="/wiki/Soil" title="Soil">soil</a> nutrient management, and disease management using <a href="/wiki/Agroforestry" title="Agroforestry">agroforestry</a> systems."<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Abdoussalami&#95;2023&#95;85-4" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Abdoussalami_2023-85"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>85<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Pests">Pests</h2></div>
<p>Bananas are damaged by a variety of pests, especially nematodes and insects.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Padmanaban-2018&#95;91-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Padmanaban-2018-91"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>91<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Nematodes">Nematodes</h3></div>
<p>Banana roots are subject to damage from multiple species of parasitic <a href="/wiki/Nematode" title="Nematode">nematodes</a>. <i><a href="/wiki/Radopholus_similis" title="Radopholus similis">Radopholus similis</a></i> causes nematode root rot, the most serious nematode disease of bananas in economic terms.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Sekora&#95;92-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Sekora-92"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>92<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Root-knot is the result of infection by species of <i><a href="/wiki/Meloidogyne" class="mw-redirect" title="Meloidogyne">Meloidogyne</a></i>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-93" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-93"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>93<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> while root-lesion is caused by species of <i><a href="/wiki/Pratylenchus" title="Pratylenchus">Pratylenchus</a></i>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Nyang&#39;au-2021&#95;94-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Nyang&#39;au-2021-94"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>94<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and spiral nematode root damage is the result of infection by <i><a href="/wiki/Helicotylenchus" title="Helicotylenchus">Helicotylenchus</a></i> species.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Zuckerman-1963&#95;95-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Zuckerman-1963-95"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>95<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-center" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Radopholus_similis.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Radopholus_similis.jpg/250px-Radopholus_similis.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="167" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f6/Radopholus_similis.jpg/500px-Radopholus_similis.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="2901" data-file-height="1933" /></a><figcaption><i><a href="/wiki/Radopholus_similis" title="Radopholus similis">Radopholus similis</a></i> inside banana root, causing nematode root rot</figcaption></figure>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Insects">Insects</h3></div>
<p>Among the main insect pests of banana cultivation are two beetles that cause substantial economic losses, the banana borer <i><a href="/wiki/Cosmopolites_sordidus" title="Cosmopolites sordidus">Cosmopolites sordidus</a></i> and the banana stem weevil <i><a href="/wiki/Odoiporus_longicollis" title="Odoiporus longicollis">Odoiporus longicollis</a></i>. Other significant pests include <a href="/wiki/Aphid" title="Aphid">aphids</a> and scarring beetles.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Padmanaban-2018&#95;91-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Padmanaban-2018-91"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>91<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size mw-halign-center" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Cosmopolites_sordidus.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Cosmopolites_sordidus.jpg/250px-Cosmopolites_sordidus.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="242" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Cosmopolites_sordidus.jpg/500px-Cosmopolites_sordidus.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="525" data-file-height="508" /></a><figcaption>The <a href="/wiki/Cosmopolites_sordidus" title="Cosmopolites sordidus">banana borer</a> is a destructive pest that tunnels inside the plant.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Padmanaban-2018&#95;91-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Padmanaban-2018-91"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>91<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></figcaption></figure>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Diseases">Diseases</h2></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/List_of_banana_and_plantain_diseases" title="List of banana and plantain diseases">List of banana and plantain diseases</a></div>
<p>Although in no danger of outright extinction, bananas of the Cavendish group, which dominate the global market, are under threat.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-NewScientist-2006&#95;96-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NewScientist-2006-96"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>96<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> There is a need to enrich banana <a href="/wiki/Biodiversity" title="Biodiversity">biodiversity</a> by producing diverse new banana varieties, not just focusing on the Cavendish.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Karp-2019&#95;97-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Karp-2019-97"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>97<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Its predecessor '<a href="/wiki/Gros_Michel" title="Gros Michel">Gros Michel</a>', discovered in the 1820s, was similarly dominant but had to be replaced after widespread infections of Panama disease. <a href="/wiki/Monocropping" title="Monocropping">Monocropping</a> of Cavendish similarly leaves it susceptible to disease and so threatens both commercial cultivation and small-scale subsistence farming.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-NewScientist-2006&#95;96-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NewScientist-2006-96"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>96<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Montpellier-2003&#95;98-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Montpellier-2003-98"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>98<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Within the data gathered from the genes of hundreds of bananas, the botanist Julie Sardos has found several wild banana ancestors currently unknown to scientists, whose genes could provide a means of defense against banana crop diseases.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-99" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-99"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>99<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Some commentators have remarked that those variants which could replace what much of the world considers a "typical banana" are so different that most people would not consider them the same fruit, and blame the decline of the banana on <a href="/wiki/Mendelian_inheritance" title="Mendelian inheritance">monogenetic</a> cultivation driven by short-term commercial motives.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-NZHerald-2008&#95;58-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NZHerald-2008-58"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>58<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Overall, <a href="/wiki/Fungal_banana_disease" class="mw-redirect" title="Fungal banana disease">fungal diseases</a> are disproportionately important to <a href="/wiki/Small_island_developing_states" class="mw-redirect" title="Small island developing states">small island developing states</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Thomas-2020&#95;100-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thomas-2020-100"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>100<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Panama_disease">Panama disease</h3></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Panama_disease_of_banana_-_vascular_decoloration_on_pseudostem.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="A banana tree cut horizontally to show the fungus development in the interior of the tree" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Panama_disease_of_banana_-_vascular_decoloration_on_pseudostem.jpg/250px-Panama_disease_of_banana_-_vascular_decoloration_on_pseudostem.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="188" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/0d/Panama_disease_of_banana_-_vascular_decoloration_on_pseudostem.jpg/500px-Panama_disease_of_banana_-_vascular_decoloration_on_pseudostem.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="720" data-file-height="540" /></a><figcaption>Panama disease <i><a href="/wiki/Fusarium" title="Fusarium">Fusarium</a></i> fungus climbing up through the banana stem </figcaption></figure>
<p><a href="/wiki/Panama_disease" title="Panama disease">Panama disease</a> is caused by a <i><a href="/wiki/Fusarium" title="Fusarium">Fusarium</a></i> soil <a href="/wiki/Fungus" title="Fungus">fungus</a>, which enters the plants through the roots and travels with water into the trunk and leaves, producing <a href="/wiki/Gel" title="Gel">gels</a> and gums that cut off the flow of water and nutrients, causing the plant to <a href="/wiki/Wilting" title="Wilting">wilt</a>, and exposing the rest of the plant to lethal amounts of sunlight. Prior to 1960, almost all commercial banana production centered on the Gros Michel cultivar, which was highly susceptible.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Barker-2008&#95;101-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Barker-2008-101"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>101<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Cavendish was chosen as the replacement for Gros Michel because, among resistant cultivars, it produces the highest quality <a href="/wiki/Fruit" title="Fruit">fruit</a>. It requires more care during shipping,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-102" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-102"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>102<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> and its quality compared to Gros Michel is debated.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Lessard-1992&#95;103-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Lessard-1992-103"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>103<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Fusarium_wilt_TR4">Fusarium wilt TR4</h4></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Tropical_Race_4" class="mw-redirect" title="Tropical Race 4">Fusarium wilt TR4</a>, a reinvigorated strain of Panama disease, was discovered in 1993. This virulent form of Fusarium wilt has destroyed Cavendish plantations in several southeast Asian countries and spread to Australia and India.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Karp-2019&#95;97-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Karp-2019-97"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>97<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> As the soil-based fungi can easily be carried on boots, clothing, or tools, the wilt spread to the Americas despite years of preventive efforts.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Karp-2019&#95;97-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Karp-2019-97"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>97<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Without genetic diversity, Cavendish is highly susceptible to TR4, and the disease endangers its commercial production worldwide.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-IBP-2012&#95;104-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-IBP-2012-104"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>104<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The only known defense to TR4 is <a href="/wiki/Plant_disease_resistance" title="Plant disease resistance">genetic resistance</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Karp-2019&#95;97-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Karp-2019-97"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>97<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This is conferred either by <a href="/wiki/RGA2" class="mw-redirect" title="RGA2">RGA2</a>, a gene isolated from a TR4-resistant <a href="/wiki/Diploid" class="mw-redirect" title="Diploid">diploid</a> banana, or by the <a href="/wiki/Nematode" title="Nematode">nematode</a>-derived Ced9.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Dale-2017&#95;105-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dale-2017-105"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>105<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-ISAAA-2021&#95;106-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ISAAA-2021-106"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>106<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This may be achieved by <a href="/wiki/Genetic_modification" class="mw-redirect" title="Genetic modification">genetic modification</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Dale-2017&#95;105-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Dale-2017-105"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>105<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-ISAAA-2021&#95;106-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ISAAA-2021-106"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>106<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Black_sigatoka">Black sigatoka</h3></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Black_Sigatoka_(detail).jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Black_Sigatoka_%28detail%29.jpg/250px-Black_Sigatoka_%28detail%29.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="159" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/20/Black_Sigatoka_%28detail%29.jpg/500px-Black_Sigatoka_%28detail%29.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="1536" data-file-height="976" /></a><figcaption>Leaf infected with <a href="/wiki/Black_sigatoka" title="Black sigatoka">black sigatoka</a> </figcaption></figure>
<p><a href="/wiki/Black_sigatoka" title="Black sigatoka">Black sigatoka</a> is a fungal leaf spot disease first observed in Fiji in 1963 or 1964. It is caused by the <a href="/wiki/Ascomycete" class="mw-redirect" title="Ascomycete">ascomycete</a> <i>Mycosphaerella fijiensis</i>. The disease, also called black leaf streak, has spread to banana plantations throughout the tropics from infected banana leaves used as packing material. It affects all main cultivars of bananas and plantains (including the Cavendish cultivars<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Holmes-2013&#95;107-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Holmes-2013-107"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>107<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>), impeding <a href="/wiki/Photosynthesis" title="Photosynthesis">photosynthesis</a> by blackening parts of the leaves, eventually killing the entire leaf. Starved for energy, fruit production falls by 50% or more, and the bananas that do grow <a href="/wiki/Ripen" class="mw-redirect" title="Ripen">ripen</a> prematurely, making them unsuitable for export. The fungus has shown ever-increasing resistance to treatment; spraying with fungicides may be required as often as 50 times a year. Better strategies, with <a href="/wiki/Integrated_pest_management" title="Integrated pest management">integrated pest management</a>, are needed.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-108" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-108"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>108<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-JGI-2013&#95;109-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-JGI-2013-109"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>109<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Banana_bunchy_top_virus">Banana bunchy top virus</h3></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Banana_aphid_colony.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="Infected Banana Plant" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Banana_aphid_colony.jpg/250px-Banana_aphid_colony.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="188" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/72/Banana_aphid_colony.jpg/500px-Banana_aphid_colony.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="1600" data-file-height="1200" /></a><figcaption>Colony of banana aphids (<i><a href="/wiki/Pentalonia_nigronervosa" title="Pentalonia nigronervosa">Pentalonia nigronervosa</a></i>), <a href="/wiki/Disease_vector" title="Disease vector">vector</a> of <a href="/wiki/Banana_bunchy_top_virus" title="Banana bunchy top virus">banana bunchy top virus</a> </figcaption></figure>
<p><a href="/wiki/Banana_bunchy_top_virus" title="Banana bunchy top virus">Banana bunchy top virus</a> is a plant virus of the genus <i>Babuvirus</i>, family <i>Nanonviridae</i> affecting <i>Musa</i> spp. (including banana, abaca, plantain and ornamental bananas) and <i>Ensete</i> spp. in the family <i>Musaceae</i>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-110" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-110"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>110<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Banana bunchy top disease symptoms include dark green streaks of variable length in leaf veins, midribs and petioles. Leaves become short and stunted as the disease progresses, becoming 'bunched' at the apex of the plant. Infected plants may produce no fruit or the fruit bunch may not emerge from the pseudostem.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Thomas-2015&#95;111-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thomas-2015-111"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>111<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The virus is transmitted by the banana aphid <i><a href="/wiki/Pentalonia_nigronervosa" title="Pentalonia nigronervosa">Pentalonia nigronervosa</a></i> and is widespread in Southeast Asia, Asia, the Philippines, Taiwan, Oceania and parts of Africa. There is no cure, but it can be effectively controlled by the eradication of diseased plants and the use of virus-free planting material.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-112" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-112"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>112<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> No resistant cultivars have been found, but varietal differences in susceptibility have been reported. The commercially important Cavendish subgroup is severely affected.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Thomas-2015&#95;111-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Thomas-2015-111"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>111<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Banana_bacterial_wilt">Banana bacterial wilt</h3></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Banana_Xanthomonas_wilt" title="Banana Xanthomonas wilt">Banana bacterial wilt</a> is a bacterial disease caused by <i><a href="/wiki/Xanthomonas_campestris" title="Xanthomonas campestris">Xanthomonas campestris</a></i> pv. <i>musacearum</i>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Tushemereirwe-2004&#95;113-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Tushemereirwe-2004-113"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>113<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> First identified on a close relative of bananas, <i><a href="/wiki/Ensete_ventricosum" title="Ensete ventricosum">Ensete ventricosum</a></i>, in Ethiopia in the 1960s,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Bradbury-1968&#95;114-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Bradbury-1968-114"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>114<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The disease was first seen in Uganda in 2001 affecting all banana cultivars. Since then it has been diagnosed in Central and East Africa, including the banana growing regions of Rwanda, the Democratic Republic of the Congo, Tanzania, Kenya, Burundi, and Uganda.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Mwangi-2007&#95;115-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mwangi-2007-115"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>115<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Conservation_of_genetic_diversity">Conservation of genetic diversity</h2></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:ITC_cold_room.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/ITC_cold_room.jpg/250px-ITC_cold_room.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="169" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/7/79/ITC_cold_room.jpg/500px-ITC_cold_room.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="5067" data-file-height="3426" /></a><figcaption>The cold storage room for the banana collection at <a href="/wiki/Bioversity_International" title="Bioversity International">Bioversity International</a>'s Musa Germplasm Transit Centre</figcaption></figure>
<p>Given the narrow range of <a href="/wiki/Genetic_diversity" title="Genetic diversity">genetic diversity</a> present in bananas and the many threats via <a href="/wiki/Biotic_component" class="mw-redirect" title="Biotic component">biotic</a> (pests and diseases) and <a href="/wiki/Abiotic_component" title="Abiotic component">abiotic</a> threats (such as <a href="/wiki/Drought" title="Drought">drought</a>) stress, <a href="/wiki/Conservation_biology" title="Conservation biology">conservation</a> of the full spectrum of banana <a href="/wiki/Plant_genetic_resources" title="Plant genetic resources">genetic resources</a> is ongoing.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Genebank-2018&#95;116-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Genebank-2018-116"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>116<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In 2024, the economist Pascal Liu of the <a href="/wiki/Food_and_Agriculture_Organization" title="Food and Agriculture Organization">FAO</a> described the impact of <a href="/wiki/Global_warming" class="mw-redirect" title="Global warming">global warming</a> as an "enormous threat" to the world supply of bananas.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-McGrath-2024&#95;117-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-McGrath-2024-117"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>117<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Banana <a href="/wiki/Germplasm" title="Germplasm">germplasm</a> is conserved in many national and regional <a href="/wiki/Gene_banks" class="mw-redirect" title="Gene banks">gene banks</a>, and at the world's largest banana collection, the International <i>Musa</i> Germplasm Transit Centre, managed by <a href="/wiki/Bioversity_International" title="Bioversity International">Bioversity International</a> and hosted at <a href="/wiki/KU_Leuven" title="KU Leuven">KU Leuven</a> in Belgium.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-BioversityInternational-2018&#95;118-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-BioversityInternational-2018-118"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>118<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Since <i>Musa</i> cultivars are mostly seedless, they are conserved by three main methods: <i><a href="/wiki/In_vivo" title="In vivo">in vivo</a></i> (planted in field collections), <i><a href="/wiki/In_vitro" title="In vitro">in vitro</a></i> (as plantlets in test tubes within a controlled environment), and by <a href="/wiki/Cryopreservation" title="Cryopreservation">cryopreservation</a> (<a href="/wiki/Meristem" title="Meristem">meristems</a> conserved in <a href="/wiki/Liquid_nitrogen" title="Liquid nitrogen">liquid nitrogen</a> at −196&#160;°C).<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Genebank-2018&#95;116-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Genebank-2018-116"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>116<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Genes from wild banana species are conserved as <a href="/wiki/DNA" title="DNA">DNA</a> and as cryopreserved <a href="/wiki/Pollen" title="Pollen">pollen</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Genebank-2018&#95;116-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Genebank-2018-116"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>116<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Seeds from wild species are sometimes conserved, although less commonly, as they are difficult to regenerate. In addition, bananas and their <a href="/wiki/Crop_wild_relatives" class="mw-redirect" title="Crop wild relatives">crop wild relatives</a> are conserved <i><a href="/wiki/In_situ" title="In situ">in situ</a></i>, in the wild natural habitats where they evolved and continue to do so. Diversity is also conserved in farmers' fields where continuous cultivation, adaptation and improvement of cultivars is often carried out by small-scale farmers growing traditional local cultivars.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-119" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-119"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>119<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Nutrition">Nutrition</h2></div>

</td></tr><tr><td colspan="2" class="infobox-full-data"><hr /><div class="wrap" style="padding:0.3em;line-height:1.2em;"><a rel="nofollow" class="external text" href="https://fdc.nal.usda.gov/food-details/1105314/nutrients">Full Link to USDA Database entry</a>
 values are for edible portion</div></td></tr><tr><td colspan="2" class="infobox-below wrap" style="background:#e0e0e0; color:inherit;padding:0.3em;line-height:1.5em;font-weight:normal"><sup>†</sup>Percentages estimated using <a href="/wiki/Reference_Daily_Intake#Daily_Values" title="Reference Daily Intake">US&#160;recommendations</a> for adults,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FDADailyValues&#95;120-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FDADailyValues-120"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>120<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> except for potassium, which is estimated based on expert recommendation from the <a href="/wiki/National_Academies_of_Sciences,_Engineering,_and_Medicine" title="National Academies of Sciences, Engineering, and Medicine">National Academies</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-NationalAcademiesPotassium&#95;121-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-NationalAcademiesPotassium-121"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>121<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup></td></tr></tbody></table>
<p>A raw banana (not including the peel) is 75% water, 23% <a href="/wiki/Carbohydrate" title="Carbohydrate">carbohydrates</a>, 1% <a href="/wiki/Protein_(nutrient)" title="Protein (nutrient)">protein</a>, and contains negligible <a href="/wiki/Fat" title="Fat">fat</a>. A reference amount of 100 grams (3.5&#160;oz) supplies 89 <a href="/wiki/Calorie" title="Calorie">calories</a>, 24% of the <a href="/wiki/Daily_Value" class="mw-redirect" title="Daily Value">Daily Value</a> of <a href="/wiki/Vitamin_B6" title="Vitamin B6">vitamin B<sub>6</sub></a>, and moderate amounts of <a href="/wiki/Vitamin_C" title="Vitamin C">vitamin C</a>, <a href="/wiki/Manganese_in_biology" title="Manganese in biology">manganese</a>, <a href="/wiki/Potassium_in_biology" title="Potassium in biology">potassium</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Dietary_fiber" title="Dietary fiber">dietary fiber</a>, with no other <a href="/wiki/Micronutrient" title="Micronutrient">micronutrients</a> in significant content (table).
</p><p>Although bananas are commonly thought to contain exceptional potassium content,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Edwards-2019&#95;122-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Edwards-2019-122"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>122<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-123" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-123"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>123<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> their actual potassium content is not high per typical food serving, having only 12% of the Daily Value for potassium (table). The potassium-content ranking for bananas among fruits, vegetables, legumes, and many other foods is medium.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-FoodDataCentralUSDA-2023&#95;124-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-FoodDataCentralUSDA-2023-124"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>124<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>

<p>Bananas are a staple <a href="/wiki/Starch" title="Starch">starch</a> for many tropical populations. Depending upon cultivar and ripeness, the flesh can vary in taste from starchy to sweet, and texture from firm to mushy. Both the skin and inner part can be eaten raw or cooked. The primary component of the aroma of fresh bananas is <a href="/wiki/Isoamyl_acetate" title="Isoamyl acetate">isoamyl acetate</a> (also known as <i>banana oil</i>), which, along with several other compounds such as <a href="/wiki/Butyl_acetate" title="Butyl acetate">butyl acetate</a> and <a href="/wiki/Isobutyl_acetate" title="Isobutyl acetate">isobutyl acetate</a>, is a significant contributor to banana flavor.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Mui-2002&#95;125-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mui-2002-125"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>125<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Plantains are eaten cooked, often as fritters.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-126" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-126"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>126<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Pisang_goreng" class="mw-redirect" title="Pisang goreng">Pisang goreng</a>, bananas fried with batter, is a popular street food in Southeast Asia.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Kraig-2013&#95;127-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kraig-2013-127"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>127<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Bananas feature in <a href="/wiki/Philippine_cuisine" class="mw-redirect" title="Philippine cuisine">Philippine cuisine</a>, with desserts like <i><a href="/wiki/Maruya_(Filipino_cuisine)" class="mw-redirect" title="Maruya (Filipino cuisine)">maruya</a></i> banana fritters.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-128" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-128"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>128<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Bananas can be made into fruit preserves.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-129" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-129"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>129<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Banana_chips" title="Banana chips">Banana chips</a> are a snack produced from sliced and fried bananas, such as in <a href="/wiki/Kerala" title="Kerala">Kerala</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Pereira-2013&#95;130-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Pereira-2013-130"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>130<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Dried bananas are ground to make <a href="/wiki/Banana_flour" title="Banana flour">banana flour</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Coghlan-2014&#95;131-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Coghlan-2014-131"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>131<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In Africa, <a href="/wiki/Matoke" title="Matoke">matoke</a> bananas are cooked in a sauce with meat and vegetables such as peanuts or beans to make the breakfast dish <a href="/wiki/Katogo_(food)" title="Katogo (food)">katogo</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-132" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-132"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>132<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In Western countries, bananas are used to make desserts such as banana bread.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-133" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-133"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>133<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<ul class="center gallery mw-gallery-nolines">
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 235px">

<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Flowers">Flowers</h4></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1314755338" /><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1311551236" /><div class="side-box side-box-right plainlinks sistersitebox"><link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1126788409" />
<div class="side-box-flex">
<div class="side-box-image"><span class="noviewer" typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Wikibooks-logo-en-noslogan.svg" class="mw-file-description"><img alt="" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Wikibooks-logo-en-noslogan.svg/40px-Wikibooks-logo-en-noslogan.svg.png" decoding="async" width="40" height="40" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Wikibooks-logo-en-noslogan.svg/60px-Wikibooks-logo-en-noslogan.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/d/df/Wikibooks-logo-en-noslogan.svg/120px-Wikibooks-logo-en-noslogan.svg.png 2x" data-file-width="400" data-file-height="400" /></a></span></div>
<div class="side-box-text plainlist">Wikibooks <a href="https://en.wikibooks.org/wiki/Cookbook" class="extiw" title="wikibooks:Cookbook">Cookbook</a> has a recipe/module on
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1126788409" /><div class="plainlist" style="margin-left: 1.6em;">
<ul><li><i><b><a href="https://en.wikibooks.org/wiki/Cookbook:Banana_Blossom" class="extiw" title="wikibooks:Cookbook:Banana Blossom"> Banana Blossom</a></b></i></li></ul>
</div></div></div>
</div>
<p>Banana flowers (also called "banana hearts" or "banana blossoms") are used as a <a href="/wiki/Vegetable" title="Vegetable">vegetable</a><sup id="cite&#95;ref-134" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-134"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>134<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> in <a href="/wiki/South_Asian_cuisine" title="South Asian cuisine">South Asian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Southeast_Asian_cuisine" class="mw-redirect" title="Southeast Asian cuisine">Southeast Asian cuisine</a>. The flavor resembles that of <a href="/wiki/Artichoke" title="Artichoke">artichoke</a>. As with artichokes, both the fleshy part of the bracts and the heart are edible.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-135" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-135"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>135<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed">
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 226px">
			<div class="thumb" style="width: 224px;"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Thanin_market_banana_flowers_and_leaves.jpg" class="mw-file-description" title="Banana flowers and leaves on sale in Thailand"><img alt="Banana flowers and leaves on sale in Thailand" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Thanin_market_banana_flowers_and_leaves.jpg/500px-Thanin_market_banana_flowers_and_leaves.jpg" decoding="async" width="224" height="150" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/fc/Thanin_market_banana_flowers_and_leaves.jpg/960px-Thanin_market_banana_flowers_and_leaves.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="3872" data-file-height="2592" /></a></span></div>
			<div class="gallerytext">Banana flowers and leaves on sale in Thailand</div>
		</li>
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 202px">
			<div class="thumb" style="width: 200px;"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Bananajf.jpg" class="mw-file-description" title="Kilawin na pusô ng saging, a Filipino dish of banana flowers"><img alt="Kilawin na pusô ng saging, a Filipino dish of banana flowers" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Bananajf.jpg/330px-Bananajf.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="150" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Bananajf.jpg/500px-Bananajf.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/53/Bananajf.jpg/960px-Bananajf.jpg 2x" data-file-width="3264" data-file-height="2448" /></a></span></div>
			<div class="gallerytext"><i>Kilawin na pusô ng saging</i>, a <a href="/wiki/Cuisine_of_the_Philippines" class="mw-redirect" title="Cuisine of the Philippines">Filipino dish</a> of banana flowers</div>
		</li>
</ul>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Leaf">Leaf</h4></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Banana_leaf" title="Banana leaf">Banana leaf</a></div>
<p>Banana leaves are large, flexible, and waterproof. While generally too tough to actually be eaten, they are often used as ecologically friendly disposable food containers or as "plates" in <a href="/wiki/South_Asia" title="South Asia">South Asia</a> and several <a href="/wiki/Southeast_Asia" title="Southeast Asia">Southeast Asian</a> countries.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-136" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-136"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>136<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Indonesian_cuisine" title="Indonesian cuisine">Indonesian cuisine</a>, banana leaf is employed in cooking methods like <a href="/wiki/Pepes" title="Pepes">pepes</a> and <a href="/wiki/Botok" title="Botok">botok</a>; banana leaf packages containing food ingredients and spices are cooked in steam or in boiled water, or are grilled on charcoal. Certain types of tamales are wrapped in banana leaves instead of corn husks.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-137" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-137"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>137<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In Malaysia, banana leaves are traditionally used as a food wrap for <a href="/wiki/Nasi_lemak" title="Nasi lemak">nasi lemak</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-malaymail&#95;138-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-malaymail-138"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>138<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> While banana leaves are not an essential ingredient, it imparts a subtle, vegetal aroma that enhances the flavor of the dish.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-139" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-139"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>139<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>When used so for steaming or grilling, the banana leaves protect the food ingredients from burning and add a subtle sweet flavor.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Morton-2013&#95;1-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Morton-2013-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/South_India" title="South India">South India</a>, it is customary to serve traditional food on a banana leaf.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-140" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-140"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>140<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In <a href="/wiki/Tamil_Nadu" title="Tamil Nadu">Tamil Nadu</a> (India), dried banana leaves are used as to pack food and to make cups to hold liquid food items.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Kora-2019&#95;141-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Kora-2019-141"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>141<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed">
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 202px">
			<div class="thumb" style="width: 200px;"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Chicken_satay_on_banana_leaf_in_Java.jpg" class="mw-file-description" title="Banana leaf as disposable plate for chicken satay in Java"><img alt="Banana leaf as disposable plate for chicken satay in Java" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Chicken_satay_on_banana_leaf_in_Java.jpg/330px-Chicken_satay_on_banana_leaf_in_Java.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="150" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Chicken_satay_on_banana_leaf_in_Java.jpg/500px-Chicken_satay_on_banana_leaf_in_Java.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/cc/Chicken_satay_on_banana_leaf_in_Java.jpg/960px-Chicken_satay_on_banana_leaf_in_Java.jpg 2x" data-file-width="1984" data-file-height="1488" /></a></span></div>
			<div class="gallerytext">Banana leaf as disposable plate for <a href="/wiki/Chicken_satay" class="mw-redirect" title="Chicken satay">chicken satay</a> in <a href="/wiki/Java" title="Java">Java</a></div>
		</li>
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 202px">
			<div class="thumb" style="width: 200px;"><span typeof="mw:File"><a href="/wiki/File:Nacatamales_in_steamer.jpg" class="mw-file-description" title="Nicaraguan Nacatamales, in banana leaves, ready to be steamed"><img alt="Nicaraguan Nacatamales, in banana leaves, ready to be steamed" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Nacatamales_in_steamer.jpg/330px-Nacatamales_in_steamer.jpg" decoding="async" width="200" height="150" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/0/03/Nacatamales_in_steamer.jpg/500px-Nacatamales_in_steamer.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/0/03/Nacatamales_in_steamer.jpg 2x" data-file-width="576" data-file-height="432" /></a></span></div>
			<div class="gallerytext">Nicaraguan <a href="/wiki/Nacatamal" title="Nacatamal">Nacatamales</a>, in banana leaves, ready to be steamed</div>
		</li>
</ul>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading4"><h4 id="Trunk">Trunk</h4></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Banana_pith" title="Banana pith">Banana pith</a></div>
<p>The tender core of the banana plant's trunk is also used in <a href="/wiki/South_Asian_cuisine" title="South Asian cuisine">South Asian</a> and <a href="/wiki/Southeast_Asian_cuisine" class="mw-redirect" title="Southeast Asian cuisine">Southeast Asian cuisine</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-142" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-142"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>142<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Examples include the Burmese dish <i><a href="/wiki/Mohinga" title="Mohinga">mohinga</a></i>, and the <a href="/wiki/Filipino_cuisine" title="Filipino cuisine">Filipino</a> dishes <i><a href="/wiki/Inubaran" title="Inubaran">inubaran</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Kadyos,_manok,_kag_ubad" title="Kadyos, manok, kag ubad">kadyos, manok, kag ubad</a></i>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Polistico-2017a&#95;143-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Polistico-2017a-143"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>143<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Polistico-2017b&#95;144-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Polistico-2017b-144"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>144<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed">
		<li class="gallerybox" style="width: 152px">

<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Paper_and_textiles">Paper and textiles</h3></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Manila_hemp" class="mw-redirect" title="Manila hemp">Manila hemp</a>, <a href="/wiki/Manila_paper" title="Manila paper">Manila paper</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Banana_paper" title="Banana paper">Banana paper</a></div>
<p>Fiber harvested from the pseudostems and leaves of the abacá banana (<i><a href="/wiki/Musa_textilis" class="mw-redirect" title="Musa textilis">Musa textilis</a></i>) and other bananas have been used for <a href="/wiki/Textile" title="Textile">textiles</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">Philippines</a> since ancient times. Archaeological evidence of cloth-weaving tools like <a href="/wiki/Spindle_whorl" title="Spindle whorl">spindle whorls</a> date back to the period between 1000 BC and 500 AD in the Philippines. However, the tropical environment and the sparsity of pre-colonial records makes it hard to trace its antiquity.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Hendrickx&#95;145-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hendrickx-145"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>145<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Blair&#95;146-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Blair-146"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>146<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Nevertheless, abacá bananas are the main source of fibers for traditional textiles still woven among various <a href="/wiki/Ethnic_groups_of_the_Philippines" class="mw-redirect" title="Ethnic groups of the Philippines">ethnic groups of the Philippines</a>. Examples of abacá-based textiles include the <i><a href="/wiki/T%27nalak" title="T&#39;nalak">t'nalak</a></i>, made by the <a href="/wiki/Tboli_people" title="Tboli people">Tiboli</a> tribe of <a href="/wiki/South_Cotabato" title="South Cotabato">South Cotabato</a>, and <i>dagmay</i>, made by the <a href="/wiki/Bagobo" class="mw-redirect" title="Bagobo">Bagobo</a> people.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-147" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-147"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>147<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Traditional abacá cloth collected from the Philippines during the <a href="/wiki/Spanish_colonial_period_of_the_Philippines" class="mw-redirect" title="Spanish colonial period of the Philippines">Spanish colonial period</a> is found in museum collections around the world, like the Boston Museum of Fine Arts and the Textile Museum of Canada.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-acl&#95;148-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-acl-148"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>148<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Banton_Burial_Cloth.JPG" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Banton_Burial_Cloth.JPG/250px-Banton_Burial_Cloth.JPG" decoding="async" width="250" height="188" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/4/4b/Banton_Burial_Cloth.JPG/500px-Banton_Burial_Cloth.JPG 1.5x" data-file-width="3968" data-file-height="2976" /></a><figcaption>The Banton Burial Cloth (<abbr title="circa">c.</abbr><span style="white-space:nowrap;">&#8201;1200–1300 AD</span>) of the <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">Philippines</a>, the oldest surviving example of banana textile (and the oldest example of warp <a href="/wiki/Ikat" title="Ikat">ikat</a> weaving in <a href="/wiki/Southeast_Asia" title="Southeast Asia">Southeast Asia</a>). It is made from <a href="/wiki/Abac%C3%A1" title="Abacá">abacá</a>,<sup id="cite&#95;ref-ocampo&#95;149-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ocampo-149"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>149<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> a species of banana endemic to the Philippines.</figcaption></figure>
<p>The oldest surviving example of textile made from banana fibers is the Banton Burial Cloth recovered from a coffin in the sacred Ipot Cave of <a href="/wiki/Banton,_Romblon" title="Banton, Romblon">Banton, Romblon</a>, <a href="/wiki/Philippines" title="Philippines">Philippines</a>, and dated to around the 13th and 14th centuries.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-ocampo&#95;149-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ocampo-149"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>149<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-ccp&#95;150-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-ccp-150"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>150<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-151" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-151"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>151<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Abacá textiles were also mentioned numerous times in Spanish colonial records in the Philippines since the 16th century, eventually acquiring the <a href="/wiki/Philippine_Spanish" title="Philippine Spanish">Philippine Spanish</a> name <span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">medriñaque</i></span> (entering the contemporary <a href="/wiki/English_language" title="English language">English language</a> as "medrinacks", "medrianacks", "medrianackes", and "medrinacles", among other names) and the English name "Manila hemp". Aside from indigenous clothing for native Filipinos, <span title="Spanish-language text"><i lang="es">medriñaque</i></span> was also used during the colonial era as <a href="/wiki/Canvas" title="Canvas">canvas</a> for sails and for stiffening clothing like skirts, collars, and <a href="/wiki/Doublet_(clothing)" title="Doublet (clothing)">doublets</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Hendrickx&#95;145-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hendrickx-145"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>145<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Craig&#95;152-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Craig-152"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>152<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Castro-Baker&#95;153-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Castro-Baker-153"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>153<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The inner fibers are also used in the making of hats, including the "Manila hats", hammocks, matting, <a href="/wiki/Cordage_(rope)" class="mw-redirect" title="Cordage (rope)">cordage</a>, ropes, coarse twines, and <a href="/wiki/Manila_paper" title="Manila paper">Manila paper</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-acl&#95;148-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-acl-148"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>148<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-154" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-154"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>154<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> By the 19th century, abacá fiber had become one of the most important economic exports of the Philippines. They were in demand due to their strength and saltwater-resistance.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-worcester&#95;155-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-worcester-155"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>155<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Outside the Philippines, abacá was first cultivated on a large scale in <a href="/wiki/Sumatra" title="Sumatra">Sumatra</a> in 1925 under the <a href="/wiki/Dutch_Empire" class="mw-redirect" title="Dutch Empire">Dutch</a>, who had observed its cultivation in the Philippines for <a href="/wiki/Cordage_(rope)" class="mw-redirect" title="Cordage (rope)">cordage</a> since the nineteenth century, followed up by plantings in <a href="/wiki/Central_America" title="Central America">Central America</a> in 1929 sponsored by the <a href="/wiki/U.S._Department_of_Agriculture" class="mw-redirect" title="U.S. Department of Agriculture">U.S. Department of Agriculture</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-eb&#95;156-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-eb-156"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>156<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> It also was transplanted into <a href="/wiki/India" title="India">India</a> and <a href="/wiki/Guam" title="Guam">Guam</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-wood&#95;157-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-wood-157"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>157<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>A similar tradition of weaving banana textiles (from <i><a href="/wiki/Musa_basjoo" title="Musa basjoo">Musa basjoo</a></i> and <i><a href="/wiki/Musa_balbisiana" title="Musa balbisiana">Musa balbisiana</a></i>) also existed among the non-Han minority groups in southern China since at least the <a href="/wiki/Han_Dynasty" class="mw-redirect" title="Han Dynasty">Han Dynasty</a> (202 BC&#160;&#8211;&#32;220 BC). Both fruit-bearing and fibrous banana species have been used.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Hendrickx&#95;145-2" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Hendrickx-145"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>145<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> This textile tradition along with the banana species <i><a href="/wiki/Musa_basjoo" title="Musa basjoo">Musa basjoo</a></i> (<a href="/wiki/Japanese_language" title="Japanese language">Japanese</a> <i>bashō</i>, 芭蕉) was introduced to the <a href="/wiki/Ryukyu_Islands" title="Ryukyu Islands">Ryukyu Islands</a> of <a href="/wiki/Japan" title="Japan">Japan</a> at around the 14th century. In the Japanese system of <a href="/wiki/Kij%C5%8Dka-bash%C5%8Dfu" title="Kijōka-bashōfu">kijōka-bashōfu</a>, leaves and shoots are cut from the plant periodically to ensure softness. Harvested shoots are first boiled in <a href="/wiki/Lye" title="Lye">lye</a> to prepare fibers for <a href="/wiki/Yarn" title="Yarn">yarn</a>-making. These banana shoots produce fibers of varying degrees of softness, yielding yarns and textiles with differing qualities for specific uses. For example, the outermost fibers of the shoots are the coarsest, and are suitable for <a href="/wiki/Tablecloth" title="Tablecloth">tablecloths</a>, while the softest innermost fibers are desirable for <a href="/wiki/Kimono" title="Kimono">kimono</a> and <a href="/wiki/Hakama" title="Hakama">kamishimo</a>. This traditional Japanese cloth-making process requires many steps, all performed by hand.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-158" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-158"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>158<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Banana_paper" title="Banana paper">Banana paper</a> can be made either from the <a href="/wiki/Bark_(botany)" title="Bark (botany)">bark</a> of the banana plant, mainly for artistic purposes, or from the fibers of the stem and non-usable fruits. The paper may be hand-made or industrially processed.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-159" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-159"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>159<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<ul class="gallery mw-gallery-packed">

<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Other_uses">Other uses</h3></div>
<p>The large leaves of bananas are locally used as <a href="/wiki/Umbrella" title="Umbrella">umbrellas</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Morton-2013&#95;1-3" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Morton-2013-1"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>1<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Banana_peel" title="Banana peel">Banana peel</a> may have capability to extract <a href="/wiki/Heavy_metal_(chemistry)" class="mw-redirect" title="Heavy metal (chemistry)">heavy metal</a> <a href="/wiki/Contamination" title="Contamination">contamination</a> from river water, similar to other <a href="/wiki/Water_purification" title="Water purification">purification</a> materials.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-160" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-160"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>160<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-161" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-161"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>161<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> Waste bananas can be used to feed <a href="/wiki/Livestock" title="Livestock">livestock</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-162" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-162"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>162<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> As with all living things, potassium-containing bananas emit <a href="/wiki/Radioactivity" class="mw-redirect" title="Radioactivity">radioactivity</a> at low levels occurring naturally from the <a href="/wiki/Potassium-40" title="Potassium-40">potassium-40</a> (K-40) isotope.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Frame-2009&#95;163-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Frame-2009-163"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>163<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The <a href="/wiki/Banana_equivalent_dose" title="Banana equivalent dose">banana equivalent dose</a> of radiation was developed in 1995 as a simple teaching-tool to educate the public about the natural, small amount of K-40 radiation occurring in everyone and in common foods.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Mansfield-1995&#95;164-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mansfield-1995-164"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>164<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Edwards-2019&#95;122-1" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Edwards-2019-122"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>122<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Potential_allergic_reaction">Potential allergic reaction</h3></div>
<p>Individuals with a <a href="/wiki/Latex_allergy" title="Latex allergy">latex allergy</a> may experience a reaction to handling or eating bananas.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-pollart&#95;165-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-pollart-165"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>165<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Taylor-2004&#95;166-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Taylor-2004-166"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>166<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading2"><h2 id="Cultural_roles">Cultural roles</h2></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:Chhat_Puja_Worship_Material.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Chhat_Puja_Worship_Material.jpg/250px-Chhat_Puja_Worship_Material.jpg" decoding="async" width="250" height="167" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9f/Chhat_Puja_Worship_Material.jpg/500px-Chhat_Puja_Worship_Material.jpg 1.5x" data-file-width="6000" data-file-height="4000" /></a><figcaption>Bananas used in <a href="/wiki/Puja_(Hinduism)" title="Puja (Hinduism)">puja</a> in the Hindu festival of <a href="/wiki/Chhath" title="Chhath">Chhath</a> in Northern India</figcaption></figure>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Arts">Arts</h3></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Edo_period" title="Edo period">Edo period</a> poet <a href="/wiki/Matsuo_Bash%C5%8D" title="Matsuo Bashō">Matsuo Bashō</a> is named after the Japanese word 芭蕉 (<span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja">Bashō</i></span>) for the <a href="/wiki/Musa_basjoo" title="Musa basjoo">Japanese banana</a>. The <span title="Japanese-language text"><i lang="ja">Bashō</i></span> planted in his garden by a grateful student became a source of inspiration to his poetry, as well as a symbol of his life and home.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-167" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-167"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>167<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The song "<a href="/wiki/Yes!_We_Have_No_Bananas" title="Yes! We Have No Bananas">Yes! We Have No Bananas</a>" was written by <a href="/wiki/Frank_Silver" title="Frank Silver">Frank Silver</a> and <a href="/wiki/Irving_Cohn" title="Irving Cohn">Irving Cohn</a> and originally released in 1923; for many decades, it was the best-selling <a href="/wiki/Sheet_music" title="Sheet music">sheet music</a> in history. Since then the song has been rerecorded several times and has been particularly popular during banana shortages.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Shaw-1987&#95;168-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Shaw-1987-168"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>168<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-oeppel-2005&#95;169-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-oeppel-2005-169"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>169<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>A person slipping on a <a href="/wiki/Banana_peel" title="Banana peel">banana peel</a> has been a staple of <a href="/wiki/Physical_comedy" title="Physical comedy">physical comedy</a> for generations. An American comedy recording from 1910 features a popular character of the time, "Uncle Josh", claiming to describe his own such incident.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-170" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-170"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>170<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>The banana's suggestively phallic shape has been exploited in artworks from Giorgio de Chirico's 1913 painting <i>The Uncertainty of the Poet</i> onwards. In 2019, an exhibition of <a href="/wiki/Natalia_LL" title="Natalia LL">Natalia LL</a>'s video and set of photographs showing a woman "sucking on a banana" at the <a href="/wiki/National_Museum_in_Warsaw" title="National Museum in Warsaw">National Museum in Warsaw</a> was taken down and the museum's director reprimanded.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Jones-2019&#95;171-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jones-2019-171"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>171<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The cover artwork for <a href="/wiki/The_Velvet_Underground_%26_Nico" title="The Velvet Underground &amp; Nico">the 1967 debut album</a> of <a href="/wiki/The_Velvet_Underground" title="The Velvet Underground">The Velvet Underground</a> features a banana made by <a href="/wiki/Andy_Warhol" title="Andy Warhol">Andy Warhol</a>. On the original vinyl LP version, the design allowed the listener to "peel" this banana to find a pink, peeled banana on the inside.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-DeMain-2011&#95;172-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-DeMain-2011-172"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>172<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> In 1989, the feminist <a href="/wiki/Guerrilla_Girls" title="Guerrilla Girls">Guerilla Girls</a> made a screenprint with two bananas, intentionally reminiscent of Warhol's, arranged to form a "0" to answer the question in the artwork, "How many works by women artists were in the Andy Warhol and Tremaine auctions at Sotheby's?".<sup id="cite&#95;ref-173" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-173"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>173<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>Italian artist <a href="/wiki/Maurizio_Cattelan" title="Maurizio Cattelan">Maurizio Cattelan</a> created a 2019 concept art piece titled <i><a href="/wiki/Comedian_(artwork)" title="Comedian (artwork)">Comedian</a></i><sup id="cite&#95;ref-174" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-174"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>174<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> involving taping a banana to a wall using silver <a href="/wiki/Duct_tape" title="Duct tape">duct tape</a>. The piece was exhibited briefly at the Art Basel in Miami before being removed from the exhibition and eaten without permission in another artistic stunt titled <i>Hungry Artist</i> by New York artist <a href="/wiki/David_Datuna" title="David Datuna">David Datuna</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-175" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-175"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>175<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Religion_and_folklore">Religion and folklore</h3></div>
<figure class="mw-default-size" typeof="mw:File/Thumb"><a href="/wiki/File:XRF-Tani-.jpg" class="mw-file-description"><img src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/XRF-Tani-.jpg/250px-XRF-Tani-.jpg" decoding="async" width="190" height="268" class="mw-file-element" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/XRF-Tani-.jpg/330px-XRF-Tani-.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/XRF-Tani-.jpg/500px-XRF-Tani-.jpg 2x" data-file-width="588" data-file-height="828" /></a><figcaption><i><a href="/wiki/Nang_Tani" title="Nang Tani">Nang Tani</a></i>, the female <a href="/wiki/Ghost" title="Ghost">ghost</a> of Thai folklore that haunts banana plants</figcaption></figure>
<p>In India, bananas serve a prominent part in many festivals and occasions of <a href="/wiki/Hindu" class="mw-redirect" title="Hindu">Hindus</a>. In <a href="/wiki/Hindu_wedding" title="Hindu wedding">South Indian weddings</a>, particularly <a href="/wiki/Tamil_culture" title="Tamil culture">Tamil weddings</a>, banana trees are tied in pairs to form an <a href="/wiki/Arch" title="Arch">arch</a> as a blessing to the couple for a long-lasting, useful life.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Indian&#95;Mirror&#95;176-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Indian_Mirror-176"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>176<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-EarthstOriez-2017&#95;177-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-EarthstOriez-2017-177"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>177<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In Thailand, it is believed that <a href="/wiki/Musa_balbisiana" title="Musa balbisiana">a certain type</a> of banana plant may be inhabited by a spirit, <a href="/wiki/Nang_Tani" title="Nang Tani">Nang Tani</a>, a type of ghost related to trees and similar plants that manifests itself as a young woman.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-178" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-178"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>178<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> People often tie a length of colored satin cloth around the pseudostem of the banana plants.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-179" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-179"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>179<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p><p>In <a href="/wiki/Ethnic_Malays" class="mw-redirect" title="Ethnic Malays">Malay folklore</a>, the ghost known as <a href="/wiki/Pontianak_(folklore)" class="mw-redirect" title="Pontianak (folklore)">Pontianak</a> is associated with banana plants (<i>pokok pisang</i>), and its spirit is said to reside in them during the day.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-180" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-180"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>180<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
<div class="mw-heading mw-heading3"><h3 id="Racial_signifier">Racial signifier</h3></div>
<link rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style" href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1320445320" /><div role="note" class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable">See also: <a href="/wiki/List_of_ethnic_slurs#Banana" title="List of ethnic slurs">List of ethnic slurs §&#160;Banana</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Racism_in_sport" title="Racism in sport">Racism in sport</a></div>
<p>In European, British, and Australian sport, throwing a banana at a member of an opposing team has long been used as a form of <a href="/wiki/Racial_abuse" class="mw-redirect" title="Racial abuse">racial abuse</a>.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Mills-2016&#95;181-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Mills-2016-181"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>181<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup><sup id="cite&#95;ref-Jackson-2014&#95;182-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Jackson-2014-182"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>182<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup> The act, which was commonplace in England in the 1980s, is meant to taunt players of <a href="/wiki/Black_African" class="mw-redirect" title="Black African">Black African</a> ancestry by equating them to apes or monkeys.<sup id="cite&#95;ref-Evans-2016&#95;183-0" class="reference"><a href="#cite_note-Evans-2016-183"><span class="cite-bracket">&#91;</span>183<span class="cite-bracket">&#93;</span></a></sup>
</p>
